Faith and Disbelief
beginner
4 min
Influence of Ibn Dahiq on the Faith of the Muqallid
Al-Sanusi's adoption of Ibn Dahiq's strict position on the muqallid's faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Sanusi adopted the strict position of Ibn Dahiq regarding the faith of the muqallid, and the distinction between worldly and afterlife judgment.

Core Idea
Al-Sanusi took the strongest view: the muqallid is not a true believer before Allah.

This lesson explores a specific doctrinal influence on Imam al-Sanusi. He adopted the most stringent position concerning the faith of a muqallid (one who follows religious authority without proof). The passage clarifies that this judgment applies in the afterlife, while in this world the muqallid is still considered a believer.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Note the key terms and the flow of the argument.
The passage includes a footnote that modifies the main claim.
2
Identify the key claim
Identify the central claim: al-Sanusi adopted the strongest opinion that the muqallid is a disbeliever. What does 'strongest' imply?
3
Understand the footnote distinction
The footnote (1) provides a crucial distinction. Read it and understand how it qualifies the claim—disbelief in Allah's sight, but belief in this world.
This distinction prevents misunderstanding about practical rulings.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this position is called 'strongest' and how it relates to the debate on taqlid. Reflect on the influence of Ibn Dahiq.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويظهر بجلاء تأثير العلامة ابن دهاق في إمامنا السنوسي في التقليد والحكم على المقلد ، فهو هنا قد أخذ بأشد الأقوال ؛ إذ قال بعدم إيمان المقلد (١) ، وشهد لذلك بمقطَّعات نقلها عن العلامة ابن دهاق (١) يعني : عند الله تعالى ، أما في الدنيا فهو مؤمن ؛ لحرمة النطق بالشهادتين
And the influence of 'Allama Ibn Dahiq upon our Imam al-Sanusi appears clearly in the matter of taqlid and the judgment on the muqallid, for he here adopted the strongest opinion; for he stated the disbelief of the muqallid (1), and he testified to that with passages he transmitted from 'Allama Ibn Dahiq. (1) Meaning: in the sight of Allah, the Exalted; as for in this world, he is a believer due to the sanctity of uttering the two testimonies.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage details a specific doctrinal influence: Ibn Dahiq's view on taqlid. Al-Sanusi adopted the harshest position — that the muqallid (one who follows authority without proof) is not a believer (in the sight of Allah). The passage notes that al-Sanusi provided evidence for this by quoting passages from Ibn Dahiq. The footnote clarifies that this refers to the afterlife; in this world, the muqallid is considered a believer due to uttering the shahada.

Key Terms
تقليد
مقلد
إيمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Sanusi held a lenient view on taqlid.
Correction: In fact, al-Sanusi adopted the strongest opinion, stating the muqallid is not a believer in Allah's sight.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what opinion did al-Sanusi adopt regarding the muqallid?
A) The muqallid is a believer in every sense.
B) The muqallid is not a believer in Allah's sight.
C) The muqallid is a sinner but still a believer.
D) The muqallid is not judged at all.

Key Takeaway
Al-Sanusi adopted the strict view that a muqallid is not a true believer in the afterlife, though still considered a believer in this world due to uttering the shahada.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your faith is built solely on taqlid—blind imitation without personal certainty—would Allah accept it? What concrete step will you take today to move from inherited belief to verified conviction?
?
Al-Sanusi held that the mere utterance of the shahada does not guarantee true faith in the afterlife. In your own life, do you rely on lip-service or have you actively sought knowledge to secure your faith with Allah?
?
The muqallid is considered a believer in this world but not in the Hereafter. How does that distinction make you feel about your own Islamic practice? Will you remain content with outward conformity, or will you pursue the deep understanding that leads to true iman?

True Faith and the Danger of False Certainty
Sanusi defines true faith and warns against being deceived by strong determination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's definition of true faith and why strong determination alone is not sufficient evidence for being upon the truth.

Core Idea
True faith is based on conclusive proofs, not on strong determination.

In this passage, Sanusi provides a clear definition of what constitutes genuine faith—it must be rooted in evidence and produce unshakable certainty. He then warns against a common error: using one's own intensity of devotion or resolve as proof of being on the right path. This lesson unpacks both the definition and the critical warning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: first a definition of true faith, then a warning against false reliance on determination.
The passage contains two main parts; identify them.
2
Examine the definition of true faith
Look at the three components: a firm statement (قول ثابت), strength of certainty (قوة يقين), and an unshakable contract (عقد راسخ). Understand that these result from conclusive proofs (قواطع البراهين).
True faith is not mere acceptance; it is evidence-based.
3
Study the warning against the muqallid
Understand why the imitator (muqallid) should not rely on his strong determination or devotion as proof of being upon the truth. Sanusi refutes this by pointing to Jews, Christians, and idolaters who also have strong determination through imitation.
The key refutation: others with false beliefs also have strong conviction.
4
Reflect on the lesson
Consider how this passage challenges the idea that sincerity or intensity of devotion is enough to guarantee truth. Ask yourself: what role do proofs play in your own beliefs?
This connects to the broader kalam emphasis on evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قول ثابت بالأدلة وقوة يقين وعقد راسخ لا يتزلزل (١) ؛ لكونه نتج عن قواطع البراهين . ولا يغتر المقلد ويستدل أنه على الحق بقوة تصميمه وكثرة تعبده ؛ للنقض عليه بتصميم اليهود والنصارى وعبدة الأوثان ومن في معناهم ؛ تقليداً لأحبارهم وآبائهم الضالين المضلين
A firm statement based on proofs, strength of certainty, and a firm contract that does not waver (1); because it results from conclusive proofs. And the imitator (muqallid) should not be deceived and use as evidence that he is upon the truth by the strength of his determination and the abundance of his devotion; because this is refuted by the determination of the Jews, Christians, and idolaters, and those like them, in imitation of their rabbis and forefathers, who are misguided and misguiding.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage defines genuine faith (قول ثابت) as that which is based on conclusive proofs (قواطع البراهين), producing certainty and firm conviction. It then warns the imitator (المقلد) against using his own strong determination and devotion as proof that he is upon the truth, because this reasoning is refuted by the fact that followers of false religions—Jews, Christians, and idolaters—also have strong determination through blind imitation of their religious leaders and ancestors. The point is that steadfastness alone is not evidence of truth; only correct evidence determines truth.

Key Terms
قول ثابت
قوة يقين
عقد راسخ
تقليد
قواطع البراهين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think that strong conviction or intense devotion is proof that one's beliefs are true.
Correction: Sanusi refutes this by showing that followers of false religions also have strong determination, so it cannot be a valid criterion.

Checkpoint
Why should the muqallid not use his strong determination as proof of being upon the truth?
A) Because determination is weak and unreliable.
B) Because followers of false religions also have strong determination.
C) Because determination is not a valid proof in any case.
D) Because determination comes from imitation, not evidence.

Key Takeaway
True faith is rooted in conclusive proofs and unshakable certainty, and strong determination alone is never evidence of truth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt absolutely certain about something in your faith, only to realize later that your certainty came from what you were taught as a child rather than from careful investigation? How do you know your current faith isn't just a well-rehearsed imitation?
?
What would remain of your worship and devotion if you lost all external supports—your community, family, or cultural environment? Are you prepared to examine whether your determination is rooted in truth or merely in comfort and habit?
?
When you notice someone from another religion displaying deep sincerity and commitment, does it ever challenge your assumption that your own convictions are automatically correct? What would it take for you to question the source of your own certainty?

The Danger of Imagination in Leading to False Beliefs
Sanusi warns against the power of imagination (wahm) in leading to shirk and innovation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how imagination (wahm) can overpower reason and lead to shirk and innovation, according to Sanusi's argument.

Core Idea
Imagination can override reason and lead to shirk.

Sanusi illustrates a profound psychological point: even when we know something is not real, our imagination can provoke fear and false beliefs. He uses the example of a rope made to look like a snake to show how imagination overcomes reason. This same mechanism, he argues, leads people to attribute power to created things and fall into shirk. The passage ends with a Quranic reminder that all contingent things are a trial.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Sanusi builds his argument from a concrete example to a general principle.
Pay attention to the shift from the rope-snake example to the broader condemnation of shirk.
2
Understand the example
Identify the example of the rope shaped like a snake. Why does a person fear it even though they know it is a rope? Sanusi says imagination (wahm) overcomes reason.
This shows how false premises can lead to emotional and behavioral responses.
3
Generalize the principle
See how Sanusi extends the example: 'By such imagination, most people have fallen into innovations and misguidance.' He lists the consequences: fixation on customs, attributing benefit/harm to creations, shirk.
The core problem is stopping at the created world and forgetting the Creator.
4
Reflect on the Quranic verse
The passage ends with Quran 2:102: 'We are only a trial, so do not disbelieve.' This reinforces that created things are tests, not objects of reliance.
Sanusi uses the verse to remind that all possible things are illusions pointing to the real goal: Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لمقدِّماتٍ تَتوهَّمُها كاذبة ، فتقولُ : هذا إنسان يمكن قيامُهُ وبطشُهُ وكلٌّ مَنْ يمكن قيامُهُ وبطشُهُ فليسَ بجمادٍ ، أو : فهو مُفزِعٌ ؛ فهذا ليس بجمادٍ ، أو : فهو مُفزِعٌ ، وكما إذا رأيت حبلاً مصنوعاً على شكل حيَّةٍ فتعلم أنَّه حبْلُ ، فإذا أُلْقِيَ عليكَ خفتَ منه ؛ لأنَّ الوهمَ (١) يغلب كثيراً على العقل ، ما قاد شيء مثل الوهم (۱) ، تقولُ النفس : هذا يشبه الحيَّةَ ، أو هذا شكل حيَّةٍ ، وكلُّ ما يكون كذلك فهو مَخُوفٌ ، أو : فالحزمُ الفِرارُ منه ، فهذا مَخُوفٌ ، أو : الحزمُ الفِرارُ منه وبمثل هذا الوهم وقعَ أكثرُ الناس في أنواع البدع والضَّلالات ، حتى وقفوا مع المعتاداتِ ، واشتغلوا بالأكوانِ عن مكوّنها ، فاعتقدوا نافعاً ما ليس بنافع ، وضاراً ما ليس بضارٍّ ، فأشركوا معَ اللهِ غيرَهُ ، وأثبتوا الوسائط بينه وبين خلقِهِ ، وأسندوا التأثير إلى ما ليس له تأثير وتوكَّلوا على مَنْ ليسَ لهُ حول ولا قُوَّةٌ ولا تدبير ولا تقدير ، ولم يعلموا أنَّ الممكناتِ كلها خيالات تنادي بلسانِ الحالِ الذي هو أفصحُ مِنْ لسانِ المقالِ مَنْ يَقِفُ عندَها وينظرُ (۲) : المقصدُ أَمامَكَ إِنَّمَا نَحْنُ فِتْنَةٌ فَلا تَكْفُرْ ﴾ [البقرة : ١٠٢] (۳)
From premises that you falsely imagine, so you say: This is a human, his standing and his strength are possible, and everyone whose standing and strength are possible is not an inanimate object, or: he is frightening; so this is not an inanimate object, or: he is frightening. And just as when you see a rope made in the shape of a snake, you know it is a rope, but if it is thrown at you, you fear it; because imagination (al-wahm) often overcomes reason. Nothing has led like imagination. The soul says: This resembles the snake, or this is the shape of a snake, and everything that is like that is fearsome, or: prudence is to flee from it, so this is fearsome, or: prudence is to flee from it. And by such imagination, most people have fallen into types of innovations (bida') and misguidances (dalalat), until they stopped at customary things, and occupied themselves with the created worlds (akwan) away from their Creator. So they believed beneficial that which is not beneficial, and harmful that which is not harmful. They associated partners with Allah, and asserted intermediaries between Him and His creation, and attributed effect to that which has no effect, and relied on one who has no power, no strength, no management, and no ordainment. They did not know that all possible things are illusions (khayalat) calling with the tongue of the state, which is more eloquent than the tongue of expression, to the one who stands before them and looks: "The goal is before you. We are only a trial, so do not disbelieve." [Quran 2:102]
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage is a warning about the power of imagination (wahm) to mislead people. The author first gives an example of false analogies based on imagined premises, showing how a rope shaped like a snake causes fear even though one knows it is a rope. He then generalizes that imagination often overcomes reason, leading to errors. By such imagination, most people fall into innovations (bida') and misguidance (dalalat). They become fixated on customary things (mu'tadat) and the created world (akwan), forgetting the Creator. Consequently, they commit shirk by attributing benefit and harm to created beings, believing in intermediaries, and relying on other than Allah. The passage ends with a Quranic verse (2:102) reminding that all contingent things are a trial and warning against disbelief.

Key Terms
الوهم (wahm)
البدع (bida')
الضلالات (dalalat)
الشرك (shirk)
المعتادات (mu'tadat)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Rational proofs alone are sufficient to prevent error and shirk.
Correction: Sanusi shows that imagination (wahm) can overpower reason, leading even those with correct knowledge to fear and false beliefs. Therefore, intellectual assent is not enough; one must also guard against the influence of imagination.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what is the primary cause of people falling into shirk and innovations?
A) Lack of rational proofs
B) Imagination overcoming reason
C) Following scripture alone
D) Ignorance of the Quran

Key Takeaway
Imagination (wahm) can overpower reason, leading people to attribute independent power to created things and commit shirk.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a specific situation where you feared or relied on something that you knew intellectually was powerless. How did your imagination override your reason, and what can you do to strengthen your reliance on Allah alone?
?
Sanusi says that by imagination, people 'stopped at customary things' and occupied themselves with creation instead of the Creator. What 'customary things' in your daily life—like routines, people, or possessions—have you come to rely on as if they have independent power? Will you commit to recognizing them as mere illusions today?
?
The verse at the end calls all possible things a 'trial so do not disbelieve.' How are the created things in your life a trial for your faith? What one action can you take this week to shift your trust from these illusions to Allah, the only true source of benefit and harm?

Faith from Certainty vs Non-Certainty
Sanusi states consensus on faith from non-certainty and knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the consensus rulings on faith based on non-certain judgments and on knowledge.

Core Idea
Faith from certainty is valid; faith from doubt is not.

Sanusi now applies the five rational judgments to the concept of faith. He begins by stating the unanimous rulings on two clear cases: faith derived from non-certainty is invalid, while faith derived from knowledge is valid. This establishes the baseline for the more nuanced discussion of belief.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the parallel structure of the two cases.
The passage uses 'fa-' to introduce each ruling.
2
Identify the two cases
The passage presents faith arising from non-certain judgments (the three types: assumption, illusion, doubt) and faith arising from knowledge (the first type of certainty).
Non-certain judgments were defined earlier.
3
Note the consensus
Sanusi says there is consensus (ijma') on both rulings. Understand that this leaves no room for disagreement on these points.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذا عرفت هذا : فالإيمان إن حصل عن أقسام غير الجزمِ الثلاثة.. فالإجماع على بطلانه ، وإن حصل عنِ القسم الأوَّلِ مِنْ قسمي الجزم ؛ وهو العلم .. فالإجماع على صحتِهِ ، وأما القسم الثاني - وهو الاعتقاد ـ فينقسم قسمين : مطابق لِمَا في نفس الأمرِ ؛ ويُسمَّى : الاعتقاد الصحيح ؛ كاعتقادِ عامة المؤمنين المقلدين ، وغيرُ مطابق ؛ ويُسمى : الاعتقاد الفاسد ، والجهل المركَّبَ ؛ كاعتقادِ الكافرين ذلكَ مِنَ فالفاسد : أجمعوا على كفرِ صاحبِهِ ، وأَنَّهُ آثم غيرُ معذورٍ ، مُخلَّدٌ في النار ، اجتهد أو قَلَّدَ ، ولا يُعتَدُّ بخلافِ مَنْ خالفَ في المبتدعة واختلفوا في الاعتقادِ الصحيح الذي حصل بمحض التقليد : فالذي عليه الجمهور والمحققونَ مِنْ أهل السنة ـ كالشيخ الأشعري والقاضي والأستاذ وإمام الحرمين وغيرهم مِنَ الأئمةِ - أَنَّهُ لا يصح الاكتفاء به في العقائدِ الدينيَّةِ ، وهو الحقُّ الذي لا شك فيه ، وقد حكى غيرُ واحدٍ الإجماع عليه ، وكأنَّهُ لم يعتد بخلافِ الحشوية وبعض أهل الظاهرِ ؛ إمَّا لظهورِ فسادِهِ وعدم متانة علم صاحبه ، أو لانعقاد إجماع السلف قبله على ضده . وحصلَ ابنُ عرفةَ في المُقلّد ثلاثة أقوال :
Now that you know this: if faith (iman) arises from the three divisions of non-certainty, then there is consensus on its invalidity. And if it arises from the first of the two divisions of certainty—which is knowledge—then there is consensus on its validity. As for the second division—which is belief (i'tiqad)—it divides into two: conforming to what is in itself; it is called correct belief, like the belief of the common believers who are blind followers (muqallidun); and non-conforming; it is called corrupt belief (al-i'tiqad al-fasid) and compounded ignorance (al-jahl al-murakkab), like the belief of the disbelievers. That from... So the corrupt: they agreed on the disbelief of its possessor, and that he is sinful, not excused, eternally in the Fire, whether he exerted effort (ijtahada) or blindly followed (qallada), and no consideration is given to the disagreement of those who disagreed among the innovators. And they differed regarding correct belief that occurred by mere blind following: the position of the majority and the investigators among Ahl al-Sunnah—like Shaykh al-Ash'ari, al-Qadi, al-Ustadh, Imam al-Haramayn, and other imams—is that it is not sufficient to rely on it in religious beliefs, and that is the truth about which there is no doubt. And more than one has narrated consensus on it, and it is as if he did not consider the disagreement of the Hashwiyya and some of the Zahiri school, either because of the manifest corruption of their view and the lack of soundness of its holder's knowledge, or because the consensus of the Salaf before him was established against it. And Ibn 'Arafa recorded three opinions regarding the blind follower:
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage is an application and argumentation passage. Sanusi applies the previously defined five judgments to the concept of faith (iman). He states that there is consensus that faith based on any of the three non-certain judgments (assumption, illusion, doubt) is invalid. Similarly, faith based on knowledge (the first type of certainty) is unanimously valid. The issue is with the second type of certainty, belief (i'tiqad), which is certainty without a compelling cause. Belief is divided into two: correct belief (conforming to reality) and corrupt belief (non-conforming, i.e., compounded ignorance). Corrupt belief, like that of disbelievers, leads to eternal damnation regardless of effort or imitation. Then Sanusi addresses the debated case: correct belief obtained through mere blind following (taqlid). The majority of Ahl al-Sunnah, including major authorities like al-Ash'ari, al-Qadi (al-Baqillani), al-Ustadh (al-Isfarayini?), and Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni), hold that such faith is not sufficient; one must have knowledge (proof). Sanusi asserts this is the undisputed truth and dismisses the contrary views of the Hashwiyya and some Zahiris as manifestly corrupt or against earlier consensus. He ends by noting that Ibn 'Arafa recorded three opinions on the blind follower, though the text cuts off here.

Key Terms
إيمان (iman)
جزم (jazm)
علم (ilm)
إجماع (ijma')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the consensus ruling on faith that arises from non-certain judgments?
A) It is valid
B) It is invalid
C) It is acceptable if accompanied by good deeds
D) It is debated

Key Takeaway
Faith based on non-certain judgments is invalid, while faith based on knowledge is unanimously valid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you base your faith on certain knowledge, or on assumptions you have never questioned? This lesson says that only knowledge is valid. Where in your faith are you relying on assumptions rather than certainty? Will you commit to seeking certainty in that area?
?
If consensus says faith from assumption is invalid, what does that mean for how you approach learning about Allah? Are you satisfied with hearsay or do you demand certain knowledge?
?
The lesson distinguishes knowledge from assumption. In your daily life, are you more driven by assumptions about Allah's attributes or by knowledge derived from revelation? What would it take for you to move from assumption to certainty?

Faith and Disbelief
intermediate
5 min
The Division of Belief and the Ruling on Corrupt Belief
Sanusi divides belief into correct and corrupt and rules on corrupt belief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how belief (i'tiqād) divides into correct and corrupt, and the severe consequences of corrupt belief.

Core Idea
Correct belief conforms to reality; corrupt belief is compounded ignorance.

Having covered the clear cases of faith from non-certainty and knowledge, Sanusi now turns to the second type of certainty: belief. He explains that belief can be either correct or corrupt, and that corrupt belief, like that of disbelievers, leads to eternal punishment regardless of effort or imitation.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification
Read how Sanusi divides belief (i'tiqad) into two types and defines each.
Note the terminology: 'i'tiqad sahih' and 'i'tiqad fasid'.
2
Understand the definitions
Correct belief conforms to reality; corrupt belief does not conform and is called compounded ignorance (jahl murakkab).
Examples: common believers (muqallidun) for correct belief; disbelievers for corrupt belief.
3
Grasp the ruling on corrupt belief
Sanusi states that the possessor of corrupt belief is a disbeliever, sinful, eternally in Hell, regardless of ijtihad or taqlid.
He dismisses contrary opinions of innovators.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذا عرفت هذا : فالإيمان إن حصل عن أقسام غير الجزمِ الثلاثة.. فالإجماع على بطلانه ، وإن حصل عنِ القسم الأوَّلِ مِنْ قسمي الجزم ؛ وهو العلم .. فالإجماع على صحتِهِ ، وأما القسم الثاني - وهو الاعتقاد ـ فينقسم قسمين : مطابق لِمَا في نفس الأمرِ ؛ ويُسمَّى : الاعتقاد الصحيح ؛ كاعتقادِ عامة المؤمنين المقلدين ، وغيرُ مطابق ؛ ويُسمى : الاعتقاد الفاسد ، والجهل المركَّبَ ؛ كاعتقادِ الكافرين ذلكَ مِنَ فالفاسد : أجمعوا على كفرِ صاحبِهِ ، وأَنَّهُ آثم غيرُ معذورٍ ، مُخلَّدٌ في النار ، اجتهد أو قَلَّدَ ، ولا يُعتَدُّ بخلافِ مَنْ خالفَ في المبتدعة واختلفوا في الاعتقادِ الصحيح الذي حصل بمحض التقليد : فالذي عليه الجمهور والمحققونَ مِنْ أهل السنة ـ كالشيخ الأشعري والقاضي والأستاذ وإمام الحرمين وغيرهم مِنَ الأئمةِ - أَنَّهُ لا يصح الاكتفاء به في العقائدِ الدينيَّةِ ، وهو الحقُّ الذي لا شك فيه ، وقد حكى غيرُ واحدٍ الإجماع عليه ، وكأنَّهُ لم يعتد بخلافِ الحشوية وبعض أهل الظاهرِ ؛ إمَّا لظهورِ فسادِهِ وعدم متانة علم صاحبه ، أو لانعقاد إجماع السلف قبله على ضده . وحصلَ ابنُ عرفةَ في المُقلّد ثلاثة أقوال :
Now that you know this: if faith (iman) arises from the three divisions of non-certainty, then there is consensus on its invalidity. And if it arises from the first of the two divisions of certainty—which is knowledge—then there is consensus on its validity. As for the second division—which is belief (i'tiqad)—it divides into two: conforming to what is in itself; it is called correct belief, like the belief of the common believers who are blind followers (muqallidun); and non-conforming; it is called corrupt belief (al-i'tiqad al-fasid) and compounded ignorance (al-jahl al-murakkab), like the belief of the disbelievers. That from... So the corrupt: they agreed on the disbelief of its possessor, and that he is sinful, not excused, eternally in the Fire, whether he exerted effort (ijtahada) or blindly followed (qallada), and no consideration is given to the disagreement of those who disagreed among the innovators. And they differed regarding correct belief that occurred by mere blind following: the position of the majority and the investigators among Ahl al-Sunnah—like Shaykh al-Ash'ari, al-Qadi, al-Ustadh, Imam al-Haramayn, and other imams—is that it is not sufficient to rely on it in religious beliefs, and that is the truth about which there is no doubt. And more than one has narrated consensus on it, and it is as if he did not consider the disagreement of the Hashwiyya and some of the Zahiri school, either because of the manifest corruption of their view and the lack of soundness of its holder's knowledge, or because the consensus of the Salaf before him was established against it. And Ibn 'Arafa recorded three opinions regarding the blind follower:
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage is an application and argumentation passage. Sanusi applies the previously defined five judgments to the concept of faith (iman). He states that there is consensus that faith based on any of the three non-certain judgments (assumption, illusion, doubt) is invalid. Similarly, faith based on knowledge (the first type of certainty) is unanimously valid. The issue is with the second type of certainty, belief (i'tiqad), which is certainty without a compelling cause. Belief is divided into two: correct belief (conforming to reality) and corrupt belief (non-conforming, i.e., compounded ignorance). Corrupt belief, like that of disbelievers, leads to eternal damnation regardless of effort or imitation. Then Sanusi addresses the debated case: correct belief obtained through mere blind following (taqlid). The majority of Ahl al-Sunnah, including major authorities like al-Ash'ari, al-Qadi (al-Baqillani), al-Ustadh (al-Isfarayini?), and Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni), hold that such faith is not sufficient; one must have knowledge (proof). Sanusi asserts this is the undisputed truth and dismisses the contrary views of the Hashwiyya and some Zahiris as manifestly corrupt or against earlier consensus. He ends by noting that Ibn 'Arafa recorded three opinions on the blind follower, though the text cuts off here.

Key Terms
اعتقاد (i'tiqad)
اعتقاد صحيح (i'tiqad sahih)
اعتقاد فاسد (i'tiqad fasid)
جهل مركب (jahl murakkab)
كفر (kufr)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that corrupt belief is only dangerous if it comes from doubt or laziness, but Sanusi says it leads to eternal punishment even if one exerted effort (ijtihad).
Correction: Corrupt belief is kufr regardless of how it was acquired; the person is not excused.

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on the possessor of corrupt belief (i'tiqad fasid) according to consensus?
A) He is sinful but not a disbeliever
B) He is a disbeliever, eternally in Hell
C) He is excused if he used ijtihad
D) His case is left to Allah

Key Takeaway
Correct belief conforms to reality; corrupt belief (compounded ignorance) makes one an eternal disbeliever, even if arrived at through effort or imitation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were to die tonight, how certain are you that your deepest convictions about God and the unseen conform to reality? Have you ever examined them with the same seriousness Sanusi demands, or have you simply inherited them without question—and if so, what will you do starting today to verify them?
?
You may have met someone who claims to have studied extensively yet arrived at a false belief about Islam. Does Sanusi's ruling that such a person is still an eternal disbeliever unsettle you? If it does, are you willing to submit to the consensus of the scholars, or do you trust your own mercy above Allah's command?
?
Sanusi dismisses the innovators' opinions as irrelevant. Are there any beliefs you hold that, if considered 'corrupt' by the mainstream, you would stubbornly defend? If so, are you prepared to abandon them for the sake of truth, even if it humbles you?

The Sufficiency of Taqlid in Correct Belief
Sanusi discusses the majority view on taqlid in correct belief and dismisses opponents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the majority position that correct belief obtained through mere imitation is insufficient, and the counterarguments.

Core Idea
Belief without proof is not enough for faith according to Ahl al-Sunnah.

The final point of this passage addresses a debated issue: is correct belief obtained through blind following sufficient for faith? Sanusi presents the position of the majority of Ahl al-Sunnah, who insist that knowledge (proof) is required, and he dismisses the opposing views of the Hashwiyya and Zahiris. He also mentions Ibn 'Arafa's three opinions on the matter.

Guided Path
1
Identify the debate
Read that scholars differed on correct belief from mere taqlid. Notice the authorities cited.
The majority includes al-Ash'ari, al-Qadi, al-Ustadh, Imam al-Haramayn.
2
Understand the majority view
The majority and investigators hold that such belief is not sufficient; one must have knowledge.
Sanusi calls this the truth without doubt.
3
Note the dismissal of opponents
Sanusi dismisses the Hashwiyya and some Zahiris, either because their view is corrupt or because earlier Salaf consensus opposes it.
4
Ibn 'Arafa's three opinions
The passage ends by noting that Ibn 'Arafa recorded three opinions on the blind follower, but they are not detailed here.
This shows the issue was not monolithic.

?

?

?

If not, what is the root cause—ignorance, negligence, or avoidance of difficult questions? The Ruling on Corrupt Belief
Sanusi states the consensus ruling on corrupt belief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the consensus ruling on corrupt belief and that it applies equally to those who arrive at it through ijtihad or taqlid.

Core Idea
Corrupt belief always leads to disbelief and eternal punishment, regardless of how it was acquired.

Sanusi now turns to the ruling on corrupt belief (al-fāsid). He reports a consensus (ijmāʿ) that the person holding such a belief is a disbeliever, sinful, and will remain forever in Hellfire. This ruling is absolute, applying whether the person arrived at the belief through personal reasoning (ijtihād) or blind imitation (taqlīd). The passage also dismisses contrary opinions from innovators as irrelevant.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the concise and definitive tone of Sanusi's statement.
The passage is a single sentence, but it contains a complete ruling.
2
Identify the four attributes of the holder of corrupt belief
The passage lists four consequences: disbelief (kufr), sinfulness (ithm), not being excused, and eternal punishment. List them and reflect on their severity.
3
Understand the universal application
Sanusi explicitly states that the ruling applies whether the person performed ijtihād or taqlīd. This closes the door to any excuse based on methodology.
This is a key point: even a sincere mujtahid is not excused if his belief is corrupt.
4
Note the dismissal of opposing views
The phrase 'no attention is paid to the opposition of those who opposed among the innovators' shows that any contrary opinion from innovators is invalid.
This reinforces the consensus and rejects flawed reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالفاسد : أجمعوا على كفرِ صاحبِهِ ، وأَنَّهُ آثم غيرُ معذورٍ ، مُخلَّدٌ في النار ، اجتهد أو قَلَّدَ ، ولا يُعتَدُّ بخلافِ مَنْ خالفَ في المبتدعة
As for the corrupt: they have consensus on the disbelief of its possessor, and that he is sinful, not excused, eternally in the Fire, whether he exerted effort (ijtahada) or imitated (qallada), and no attention is paid to the opposition of those who opposed among the innovators.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage gives the ruling for corrupt belief. There is consensus (ijmāʿ) that the person holding corrupt belief is a disbeliever (kāfir), sinful (āthim), not excused (ghayru maʿdhūr), and will remain eternally in the Fire (mukhalled fī al-nār). This applies whether the person arrived at the belief through personal effort (ijtihād) or through blind imitation (taqlīd). The opposition of certain innovators (al-mubtadiʿa) who disagree is not considered valid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think that a person who follows taqlīd in matters of corrupt belief might be excused because they did not know better.
Correction: The text explicitly states that both the mujtahid and the muqallid are equally sinful and unexcused. The ruling is absolute.

Checkpoint
According to the consensus mentioned by Sanusi, what is the fate of the holder of corrupt belief?
A) He is sinful but may be forgiven if he repents
B) He is a disbeliever eternally in the Fire
C) He is excused if he followed taqlīd
D) He is not a disbeliever if he exerted ijtihād

Key Takeaway
The possessor of corrupt belief is a disbeliever, sinful, not excused, and eternally in Hellfire, whether by ijtihad or taqlid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you willing to examine every belief you hold, even those you inherited or arrived at through your own reasoning, and submit to the consensus of Ahl al-Sunnah—even if it means admitting you were wrong and risking the label of disbelief?
?
Have you ever excused a false belief in yourself or others because it came from sincere effort or a trusted teacher? How will you now guard your heart against this dangerous assumption, knowing the words 'not excused' apply to you?
?
If you were to die tonight holding a corrupt belief, would you be among the eternally punished? What concrete step will you take today to ensure your creed is correct according to the scholars of Ahl al-Sunnah, and not merely your own opinion?

beginner

The Majority View on Taqlid and Correct Belief
Sanusi states the majority rejection of taqlid in beliefs and dismisses opposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the majority of Ahl al-Sunna reject pure taqlid as sufficient for religious beliefs and how they dismiss opposing views.

Core Idea
Correct belief requires more than blind imitation; the Salaf consensus opposes taqlid in matters of creed.

Before we examine specific opinions on the muqallid, we need to understand the mainstream position. Sanusi clarifies that the majority of scholars, including the great imams, consider pure taqlid insufficient for religious beliefs. This sets the stage for the debate on whether such a person is excused or sinful.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage carefully, then its translation. Notice the strong language: 'the truth about which there is no doubt'.
2
Identify the key scholars mentioned
List the scholars Sanusi cites: al-Ashʿarī, al-Qāḍī, al-Ustādh, Imām al-Ḥaramayn. They represent the authoritative chain of Sunni kalam.
3
Understand the dismissal of opposition
Sanusi mentions that the opposition of the Ḥashwiyya and some Ẓāhirīs is disregarded. Reflect on why their views are considered corrupt or overridden by Salaf consensus.
This shows the weight of scholarly consensus in creedal matters.
4
Connect to the bigger picture
This lesson establishes that taqlid alone is not a valid basis for faith, which raises the question: what about those who only have taqlid? That will be addressed later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واختلفوا في الاعتقادِ الصحيح الذي حصل بمحض التقليد : فالذي عليه الجمهور والمحققون مِنْ أهل السنة ـ كالشيخ الأشعري والقاضي والأستاذ وإمام الحرمين وغيرهم مِنَ الأئمةِ - أَنَّهُ لا يصح الاكتفاء به في العقائدِ الدينيَّةِ ، وهو الحقُّ الذي لا شك فيه ، وقد حكى غيرُ واحدٍ الإجماع عليه ، وكأنَّهُ لم يعتد بخلافِ الحشوية وبعض أهل الظاهرِ ؛ إمَّا لظهورِ فسادِهِ وعدم متانة علم صاحبه ، أو لانعقاد إجماع السلف قبله على ضده . وحصلَ ابنُ عرفةَ في المُقلّد ثلاثة أقوال :
الأول : أنَّه مؤمن غيرُ عاص بتركِ النظر ، الثاني : أنه مؤمن لكنه عاص إن ترك النظرَ معَ القدرةِ ، الثالثُ : أَنَّهُ كافر .
نصُّهُ في «شاملِهِ» الذي حاذى به « طوالع البيضاوي » : التقليد : اعتقاد جازمٌ لقول غير معصوم ، فيخرج : اعتقاد قولِ الرسول ، والإجماع ، ومعرفة مدلول الشهادتين ، والمعادِ والفتنة ؛ إما بدليل إجمالي معجوزٍ عن تقريرِهِ وحل شُبَهِهِ ، أو تفصيلي مقدور عليهما فيه
And they disagreed concerning correct belief that occurs through pure taqlīd: what the majority and the investigators of Ahl al-Sunna – like al-Shaykh al-Ashʿarī, al-Qāḍī, al-Ustādh, Imām al-Ḥaramayn, and others from the imāms – hold is that it is not valid to suffice with it in religious beliefs, and it is the truth about which there is no doubt. And more than one has related consensus upon it, and it is as if he did not consider the opposition of the Ḥashwiyya and some of the Ẓāhirīs; either because of the obvious corruption of [their view] and the lack of soundness of their knowledge, or because of the consensus of the Salaf before him to the contrary. And Ibn ʿArafa recorded concerning the muqallid three opinions: First: that he is a believer not sinful for leaving nazar; second: that he is a believer but sinful if he leaves nazar while having ability; third: that he is a disbeliever. His text in his “Shāmil”, with which he emulated the “Ṭawāliʿ al-Bayḍāwī”: taqlīd is a firm belief in the statement of a non-infallible, so there is excluded: belief in the statement of the Messenger, and consensus, and knowledge of the import of the two shahādas, and the Resurrection and the trial; either by a general proof that he is incapable of articulating and resolving its ambiguities, or a detailed proof that he is capable of both concerning it.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage deals with the validity of correct belief acquired through pure taqlīd (imitation). The majority and investigators of Ahl al-Sunna, including prominent figures like al-Ashʿarī, al-Qāḍī (Abū Bakr al-Bāqillānī?), al-Ustādh (Abū Isḥāq al-Isfarāyīnī), and Imām al-Ḥaramayn (al-Juwaynī), hold that taqlīd alone is not sufficient for religious beliefs. This is asserted as the truth without doubt, and several scholars have claimed consensus on this point. The opposition of the Ḥashwiyya (anthropomorphists) and some Ẓāhirīs (literalists) is disregarded because their view is patently corrupt or because the Salaf were already in consensus against them. Then Ibn ʿArafa is cited as recording three opinions regarding the muqallid (one who follows taqlīd):
1. He is a believer and not sinful for abandoning nazar (rational investigation).
2. He is a believer but sinful if he abandons nazar while capable.
3. He is a disbeliever.
Finally, the Shāmil defines taqlīd as a firm belief (iʿtiqād jāzim) in the statement of a non-infallible (ghayr maʿṣūm). This definition excludes belief in the statement of the Messenger, consensus, knowledge of the meaning of the two shahādahs, Resurrection, and the trial (fitnat al-qabr), whether acquired through a general proof that one cannot articulate or a detailed proof that one can.

Key Terms
تقليد
اعتقاد صحيح
إجماع
حشوية
ظاهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority opinion, what is the ruling on pure taqlīd in religious beliefs?
A) It is sufficient and valid
B) It is not sufficient
C) It is sinful
D) It is recommended

Key Takeaway
The majority of Ahl al-Sunna hold that correct belief cannot be based solely on taqlid; this view is supported by consensus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you hold any belief about Allah simply because your parents or community taught it, without ever personally investigating its proofs? What would it mean for your soul if that belief were incorrect?
?
When you face a doubt about a matter of creed, do you actively seek evidence from revelation and reason, or do you rely on 'what you've always heard'? Are you ready to be held accountable for that choice?
?
Imagine standing before Allah and He asks: 'Did you believe in Me because you knew, or because you followed others?' What will your answer be?

Three Opinions on the Muqallid and Definition of Taqlid
Ibn Arafa's three opinions on the muqallid and the definition of taqlid from the Shamil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three opinions regarding a person who believes through taqlid and understand the precise definition of taqlid.

Core Idea
Scholars disagree on the muqallid's status, but taqlid itself means firm belief in a non-infallible's statement.

Now that we know the majority view that taqlid alone is insufficient, the question becomes: what is the actual status of a person who believes through taqlid? Ibn Arafa recorded three distinct opinions. Moreover, we need a clear definition of taqlid itself, which Sanusi provides from the Shamil.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation for the three opinions
Read the part from 'وحصلَ ابنُ عرفةَ' through the three numbered opinions. Notice each opinion varies in severity.
2
Analyze the three opinions
List the three opinions: (1) believer, no sin; (2) believer, sinful if able to investigate; (3) disbeliever. Reflect on the reasoning behind each.
3
Read the definition of taqlid
Now read the definition from the Shamil. Note that it is 'a firm belief in the statement of a non-infallible'. This is important for understanding what taqlid is not.
4
Consider the exclusions
The definition excludes belief in the statement of the Messenger, consensus, and knowledge of the two shahadas and the afterlife. These must be based on proof, not taqlid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واختلفوا في الاعتقادِ الصحيح الذي حصل بمحض التقليد : فالذي عليه الجمهور والمحققون مِنْ أهل السنة ـ كالشيخ الأشعري والقاضي والأستاذ وإمام الحرمين وغيرهم مِنَ الأئمةِ - أَنَّهُ لا يصح الاكتفاء به في العقائدِ الدينيَّةِ ، وهو الحقُّ الذي لا شك فيه ، وقد حكى غيرُ واحدٍ الإجماع عليه ، وكأنَّهُ لم يعتد بخلافِ الحشوية وبعض أهل الظاهرِ ؛ إمَّا لظهورِ فسادِهِ وعدم متانة علم صاحبه ، أو لانعقاد إجماع السلف قبله على ضده . وحصلَ ابنُ عرفةَ في المُقلّد ثلاثة أقوال :
الأول : أنَّه مؤمن غيرُ عاص بتركِ النظر ، الثاني : أنه مؤمن لكنه عاص إن ترك النظرَ معَ القدرةِ ، الثالثُ : أَنَّهُ كافر .
نصُّهُ في «شاملِهِ» الذي حاذى به « طوالع البيضاوي » : التقليد : اعتقاد جازمٌ لقول غير معصوم ، فيخرج : اعتقاد قولِ الرسول ، والإجماع ، ومعرفة مدلول الشهادتين ، والمعادِ والفتنة ؛ إما بدليل إجمالي معجوزٍ عن تقريرِهِ وحل شُبَهِهِ ، أو تفصيلي مقدور عليهما فيه
And they disagreed concerning correct belief that occurs through pure taqlīd: what the majority and the investigators of Ahl al-Sunna – like al-Shaykh al-Ashʿarī, al-Qāḍī, al-Ustādh, Imām al-Ḥaramayn, and others from the imāms – hold is that it is not valid to suffice with it in religious beliefs, and it is the truth about which there is no doubt. And more than one has related consensus upon it, and it is as if he did not consider the opposition of the Ḥashwiyya and some of the Ẓāhirīs; either because of the obvious corruption of [their view] and the lack of soundness of their knowledge, or because of the consensus of the Salaf before him to the contrary. And Ibn ʿArafa recorded concerning the muqallid three opinions: First: that he is a believer not sinful for leaving nazar; second: that he is a believer but sinful if he leaves nazar while having ability; third: that he is a disbeliever. His text in his “Shāmil”, with which he emulated the “Ṭawāliʿ al-Bayḍāwī”: taqlīd is a firm belief in the statement of a non-infallible, so there is excluded: belief in the statement of the Messenger, and consensus, and knowledge of the import of the two shahādas, and the Resurrection and the trial; either by a general proof that he is incapable of articulating and resolving its ambiguities, or a detailed proof that he is capable of both concerning it.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage deals with the validity of correct belief acquired through pure taqlīd (imitation). The majority and investigators of Ahl al-Sunna, including prominent figures like al-Ashʿarī, al-Qāḍī (Abū Bakr al-Bāqillānī?), al-Ustādh (Abū Isḥāq al-Isfarāyīnī), and Imām al-Ḥaramayn (al-Juwaynī), hold that taqlīd alone is not sufficient for religious beliefs. This is asserted as the truth without doubt, and several scholars have claimed consensus on this point. The opposition of the Ḥashwiyya (anthropomorphists) and some Ẓāhirīs (literalists) is disregarded because their view is patently corrupt or because the Salaf were already in consensus against them. Then Ibn ʿArafa is cited as recording three opinions regarding the muqallid (one who follows taqlīd):
1. He is a believer and not sinful for abandoning nazar (rational investigation).
2. He is a believer but sinful if he abandons nazar while capable.
3. He is a disbeliever.
Finally, the Shāmil defines taqlīd as a firm belief (iʿtiqād jāzim) in the statement of a non-infallible (ghayr maʿṣūm). This definition excludes belief in the statement of the Messenger, consensus, knowledge of the meaning of the two shahādahs, Resurrection, and the trial (fitnat al-qabr), whether acquired through a general proof that one cannot articulate or a detailed proof that one can.

Key Terms
مقلد
نظر
تقليد
إجمالي
تفصيلي
شامل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Taqlid is always blind imitation and therefore always sinful.
Correction: The first opinion shows that some scholars consider the muqallid a believer without sin; the matter is disputed.

Checkpoint
According to the definition in the Shāmil, what is excluded from the scope of taqlīd?
A) Belief in the statement of a non-infallible
B) Belief in the statement of the Messenger, consensus, and knowledge of the two shahādahs
C) Only belief in the statement of the Messenger
D) Belief in the resurrection and the trial

Key Takeaway
The status of the muqallid is disputed among three opinions, but taqlid itself is narrowly defined as firm belief in a non-infallible's statement.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your belief in the shahada and the afterlife: is it based on taqlid of your parents or a detailed proof you can articulate? If taqlid excludes these core beliefs, are you actually a believer in the full sense as defined by this lesson? What would you need to change?
?
Ibn Arafa records an opinion that the muqallid is a disbeliever. Does that possibility disturb you? Are you comfortable with your level of certainty regarding the foundations of your faith?
?
The definition of taqlid requires a firm belief in a non-infallible's statement. Whose statements do you believe firmly? Are they infallible? What does it mean for your trust in scholars?

Knowledge Is Not Blamed for the Sins of Some Scholars
Sanusi refutes the claim that taqlid is sufficient because some scholars sin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the sins of some scholars do not diminish the obligation and honor of knowledge, and why mere imitation (taqlid) is not praiseworthy.

Core Idea
Knowledge is honorable by itself; sin comes from lack of divine success, not from knowledge.

This passage addresses a common objection: if some scholars sin, why is knowledge obligatory while imitation (taqlid) is not? Sanusi explains that the value of knowledge is independent of the actions of its bearers. The key insight is that knowledge and action are not necessarily linked; the real cause of righteousness is divine success (tawfiq).

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and its premise
Notice that the opponent argues that some imitators are more pious than some scholars, so taqlid should be sufficient. Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully.
The passage begins with 'وإن أراد بالإيمان...'
2
Identify Sanusi's concession
Sanusi grants the factual claim about behavior ('فمسلم'), but then introduces a crucial distinction: benefiting from knowledge is in Allah's hands, not automatic.
The phrase 'الانتفاع بالعلم بيد الله' is key.
3
Understand the logical distinction
Follow the reasoning: there is no rational connection between knowledge and action. Therefore, a scholar's sin does not tarnish knowledge, nor does an imitator's piety elevate taqlid.
'ليس بين العلم والعمل ربط عقلي'
4
Grasp the role of tawfiq
The passage concludes that knowledge leads to compliance only when accompanied by divine success. This explains why some scholars fail to act on their knowledge.
'بل إنما يحمل العلم في الحقيقة لو صاحبه التوفيق على الموافقة'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن أراد بالإيمان ما ينشأ عنه من أعمال البر ، وأن بعض المقلدين يتحفظ من المعاصي ، ويلتزم من القيام بالأوامر ما لا يوجد في كثير من العلماء .. فمسلم؛ لأن الانتفاع بالعلم بيد الله ، وليس بين العلم والعمل ربط عقلي ، إلا أن هذا لا يقدح في وجوب العلم ولا في شرفه ، وليس العلم هو الذي حمل العالم على المخالفة حتى يقدح في شرفه، ولا التقليد هو الذي حمل المقلد على الموافقة حتى يدعي شرفه، بل إنما يحمل العلم في الحقيقة لو صاحبه التوفيق على الموافقة .
And if he means by faith what arises from it of righteous deeds, and that some blind followers (muqallidun) refrain from sins and adhere to performing commands more than is found in many of the scholars... then it is granted; because benefiting from knowledge is in the hand of Allah, and there is no rational connection between knowledge and action. However, this does not detract from the obligation of knowledge nor from its honor. And it is not knowledge that led the scholar to opposition so that it detracts from its honor, nor is it blind following that led the muqallid to compliance so that he can claim its honor. Rather, knowledge truly only leads to compliance if accompanied by divine success (tawfiq).
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage responds to an objection: some argue that mere imitation (taqlid) is acceptable because many scholars sin while some imitators do good deeds. The author grants that outward actions may differ, but this does not diminish the obligation and honor of knowledge. He explains that benefiting from knowledge is by Allah's will, not by a necessary link between knowledge and action. Knowledge itself is not the cause of sin; rather, it is the lack of divine success (tawfiq) that leads a scholar to sin. Similarly, taqlid is not the cause of the imitator's good deeds. Thus, the existence of sinful scholars does not make taqlid praiseworthy or knowledge blameworthy.

Key Terms
الإيمان
أعمال البر
المقلدين
التقليد
العلم
العمل
التوفيق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if a scholar sins, his knowledge is worthless or blameworthy.
Correction: Sanusi argues that knowledge remains honorable; sin is due to lack of divine success (tawfiq), not knowledge itself.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, why does a scholar sometimes sin despite having knowledge?
A) Knowledge itself causes sin.
B) The scholar lacks divine success (tawfiq).
C) The scholar is not truly knowledgeable.
D) Sinning is a necessary part of being a scholar.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge is not blamed for the sins of its possessors; the real cause of action is divine success (tawfiq).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a scholar who sins, do you feel justified in abandoning the pursuit of knowledge? How does this passage challenge that feeling? What concrete step can you take this week to honor knowledge even when its bearers fall short?
?
Reflect on your own actions: Are you sometimes more pious in following rituals than in seeking understanding? Ask yourself: Is your piety due to tawfiq from Allah, or are you relying on your own efforts? How can you increase your reliance on Allah for tawfiq?
?
This passage says benefiting from knowledge is in Allah's hands. Have you ever felt proud of your knowledge as if you earned it yourself? What does this passage demand of you in terms of humility and dependence on Allah?

Fakhr al-Din's Deathbed Regret: A Defense
Author interprets Fakhr al-Din's deathbed wish to be a muqallid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends Fakhr al-Din al-Razi by interpreting his deathbed statement as a result of fear and doubt, not as a rejection of rational theology.

Core Idea
Fakhr al-Din's regret at death was a fear-driven retreat, not a repudiation of kalam.

This passage examines a famous anecdote about Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, the great Ash'ari theologian, who reportedly wished at death to be among those with simple imitative faith. The author, a later scholar, does not see this as a condemnation of theology but as a sign of the overwhelming fear of doubt that can assail even the greatest minds. By analyzing the context and the verses Razi recited, the author offers a sympathetic reading that turns a potential criticism into a lesson about the limits of human intellect.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once for its tone, then the translation to grasp the full argument. Notice the author's personal interjection ('I say') which signals his defense.
The passage includes a hostile quote first, then the author's counter.
2
Identify the two interpretations
The author offers two possible meanings for Fakhr al-Din's wish: a literal desire to be among imitators due to fear, or an expression of regret. Also note the alternative reading of 'old women' as those with minimal correct belief.
The poem is key to understanding Razi's state of mind.
3
Ask: Why does the author defend Razi?
Consider that the author likely wants to preserve the reputation of a major scholar and show that even the most rigorous theologian can face existential doubt at life's end. This reveals the human side of ilm al-kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : وكانَ بيدِهِ قضيب ، فقال : لو أدركتُ فخر الدينِ لَضربتُهُ بقضيبي هذا على رأسه ) انتهى (۱) قلتُ : فلعل الفخرَ  َرحمَهُ اللهُ تعالى حضرَ لهُ عندَ المَوتِ مِنَ الشُّبَهِ التي عَسُرَ عليه الانفصال عنها .. ما حمله الخوفُ منهُ أنْ تَمَنَّى أن يكونَ [ من الطويل] في درجة الاعتقادِ التقليدي ، لأنَّ رأيه فيهِ أنَّهُ كافٍ ، وقد رُوِيَ عنهُ أَنَّهُ أنشد عند الموتِ (۲) : نِهَايَةُ أَقْدَامِ (۳) الْعُقُولِ عِقَالُ وَأَكْثَرُ سَعْيِ الْعَالِمِينَ ضَلَالُ وَأَرْوَاحُنَا فِي وَحْشَةٍ مِنْ جُسُومِنَا وَحَاصِلُ دُنْيَانَا أَذِيَّ وَوَبَالُ وَلَمْ نَسْتَفِدْ مِنْ بَحْثِنَا طُولَ عُمْرِنَا سِوَى أَنْ جَمَعْنَا فِيهِ قِيلَ وَقَالُوا وَكَمْ مِنْ رِجَالٍ قَدْ رَأَيْنَا وَدَوْلَةٍ فَبَادُوا جَمِيعاً مُسْرِعِينَ وَزَالُوا وَكَمْ مِنْ جِبَالٍ قَدْ عَلَتْ شُرُفَاتِهَا رِجَالٌ فَمَاتُوا وَالْجِبَالُ جِبَالُ فعلى هذا الاحتمال يكونُ الفخرُ تمنَّى لِعِظَمِ الخوفِ الدخول في حرْزِ المقلدين حقيقةً ، أو على معنى التلهف والندم على ما فاتَ ويحتملُ أنْ يكونَ معَ هاذا أرادَ بالعجائز العجائز المقتصراتِ على القَدْرِ الضروري في تصحيح العقائدِ ؛ إِذْ هو حال عجائز ذلكَ الزمانِ وما قبلَهُ مِنَ الأزمنة الفاضلة كما قدَّمنا (٤)
He said: 'And in his hand was a stick, and he said: If I had met Fakhr al-Din I would have struck him with this stick on his head.' End. I say: Perhaps Fakhr al-Din – may God Almighty have mercy on him – at death encountered doubts from which it was difficult for him to disengage, and fear drove him to wish to be in the degree of imitative belief, because in his view it was sufficient. And it was narrated from him that he recited at death: 'The ultimate goal of the intellects is a fetter, and most of the striving of the scholars is misguidance. And our souls are in solitude from our bodies, and the result of our world is injury and calamity. And we have not benefitted from our research throughout our lives except that we have gathered in it 'they said' and 'they said'. And how many men and a state we have seen, then they all perished quickly and vanished. And how many mountains have been topped by men, then they died while the mountains remained mountains.' So on this possibility, Fakhr al-Din wished, due to the greatness of fear, to enter the fortress of the imitators literally, or as an expression of regret and remorse over what was missed. And it is possible that by 'old women' he meant the old women who restrict themselves to the essential amount for correcting beliefs, for that was the state of the old women of that time and the virtuous ages before it, as we have previously mentioned.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage is an interpretive analysis of a reported statement about Fakhr al-Din al-Razi. The author begins by quoting someone who said they would have struck Fakhr al-Din with a stick if they had met him – a clearly hostile remark. The author then interjects ('I say') to offer a charitable explanation. He suggests that perhaps at death Fakhr al-Din faced doubts (shubuhat) from which he could not extricate himself, and out of fear he wished to be among those with simple imitative faith (taqlid), because in his own view that level of faith was sufficient. To support this, the author quotes verses recited by Fakhr al-Din at death, which express despair at intellectual striving and imply that the certainties of simple faith are preferable. The author then analyzes the meaning of this wish: either it was a literal desire to enter the 'fortress of imitators' due to overwhelming fear, or it was an expression of regret. He also offers an alternative interpretation of the word 'old women' (al-‘ajā’iz) – that Fakhr al-Din meant those women who limit themselves to the minimum essential for correct belief, which was the common state of women in earlier virtuous times. The author thus defends Fakhr al-Din against criticism by explaining his deathbed statement as a result of fear and doubt, not as a rejection of rational theology.

Key Terms
تقليد
شبهات
عجائز
اعتقاد تقليدي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the author is criticizing Fakhr al-Din al-Razi or that Razi rejected rational theology on his deathbed.
Correction: The author is actually defending Razi by explaining his deathbed statement as a result of fear and doubt, not as a repudiation of kalam. He offers a generous interpretation consistent with Razi's overall view that imitative belief is sufficient.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why did Fakhr al-Din al-Razi wish to be among the muqallidin at his death?
A) Because he abandoned theology and became a simple believer.
B) Because he faced doubts that were hard to resolve and feared for his faith.
C) Because he always preferred taqlid over rational inquiry.
D) Because he was coerced by those around him.

Key Takeaway
Fakhr al-Din al-Razi's deathbed wish for imitative faith was a fear-driven response to unanswerable doubts, not a rejection of rational theology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your lifetime of intellectual pursuit suddenly felt hollow at the moment of death, what would remain of your faith? Would you, like Razi, wish for the simple certainty of the 'old women'?
?
Is your current faith built on the solidity of rational proofs or on a living relationship with the One who is beyond all proof? If the proofs crumbled, would you still be certain?
?
Razi's poem says: 'The ultimate goal of the intellects is a fetter.' Has your own reasoning ever become a chain that prevents you from surrendering to God? What would it cost you to let go of your need to understand everything?

The Unsafety of Taqlid in the Present Era
Author laments that even taqlid is no refuge due to neglect of teaching

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author considers even imitative faith (taqlid) an unreliable refuge in his time, due to widespread neglect of teaching Islamic beliefs.

Core Idea
When teaching is abandoned, even taqlid becomes unsafe.

In the previous section, Sanusi discussed a possible comfort for Fakhr al-Din through imitative faith. Now he turns to his own era and laments that even that minimal refuge is no longer available. The reason is stark: the basic teaching of beliefs has been so neglected that people cannot even imitate properly. This passage paints a vivid picture of religious ignorance spreading from slaves to scholars.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise
The author says taqlid is no longer a refuge. Why? Because there is no proper teaching of beliefs. So even imitation is not working.
2
Identify the groups affected
He specifically mentions women, children, slaves, and even those who claim knowledge. Notice the escalation from the most vulnerable to the supposedly learned.
3
Examine the description of minds
The author describes the mental state of his contemporaries as rigid, unteachable, and if they learn, they misuse knowledge. Reflect on why this is a warning.
4
Connect to prophecies
The author explicitly links this time to hadith warnings and the fear of the righteous predecessors. This adds a sense of urgency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبهذا تعرفُ أنَّ هذا الحرزَ في زماننا ليس بمأمون ؛ إذْ لا إتقان فيه للعقائد ولو بالتقليد ، فلا مدخل له في ذلك الأمرِ ؛ لعدم الاعتناء بتعليم عقائدِ الدين لا سيما النساء والصبيانِ ، أمَّا الإماء والعبيد في زماننا فلا يُقصدونَ بتعليم أصلاً ، وكأنَّهم عندَ مُلاكهم حيوان بهيمي لا تكليف عليهم ! ولهذا تجد الجهل بكثيرٍ مِنَ العقائدِ في كثيرٍ ممَّنْ يتعاطى العلمَ مِنْ أَهلِ زماننا ، فكيفَ بالعامَّةِ ؟! فكيفَ بالنساء والصبيانِ ؟! فكيف بالإماء والعبيد ؟! أمَّا أهل الباديةِ ومَنْ بَعُدَ عن سماع مطلق العلم.. فلا تسأل عن حالهم ! وتجد أذهان أكثر أهل هذا الزمانِ جامدة صعبة الانقياد للفهم مائلة أبداً لِمَا لا يعني ؛ إنْ نُصِحَتْ لم تقبل ، وإِنْ عُلِّمَتْ لم تتعلَّمْ وإِنْ فُهَمَتْ لم تفهمْ ، وإِنْ فَهِمَتْ تَفَلَّتَ منها فهمُها عن قُرْب (۱) ، وإِنْ بقي شيء منهُ بَطِرَتْ وجعلَتْهُ سُلَّماً للدنيا ولصحبةِ الظُّلَمَةِ والتقرب إليهم ، إلا مَنْ عصمَهُ اللهُ بفضلِهِ ، وما أندر وجودَهُ اليوم ! ولا حول ولا قوة إلا بالله وبالجملة : فهذا الزمانُ هو الذي هُوّلَ أمره فى الأحاديث ، وحَذَرَ منه السلف الصالح ، وخافوا أن يدركوه على غزارة علمهم وقُوَّةِ دينهم ؛ وها نحنُ أدركناهُ معَ شِدَّةِ ضَعفنا علماً وديناً المستعان (۲) والله
And by this you know that this refuge in our time is not secure, because there is no mastery of beliefs in it even by imitation, so there is no entry for it in that matter, due to the lack of care in teaching the beliefs of the religion, especially women and children. As for slave-women and slaves in our time, they are not intended for teaching at all, as if in the view of their owners they are animal cattle with no obligation upon them! Because of this you find ignorance of many beliefs among many of those who pretend to knowledge from the people of our time, then how about the common folk?! Then how about women and children?! Then how about slave-women and slaves?! As for the people of the desert and those who are far from hearing any knowledge at all – do not ask about their state! And you find the minds of most people of this time rigid, difficult to submit to understanding, always inclining to what does not concern them; if they are advised, they do not accept; if they are taught, they do not learn; if they are made to understand, they do not comprehend; and if they understand, their understanding quickly slips away; and if anything of it remains, they become arrogant and make it a ladder for worldly gain and for the company of oppressors and seeking closeness to them, except those whom God protects by His grace – and how rare is his existence today! And there is no power or strength except by God. In summary, this is the time whose matter was made great in the hadiths, and the righteous predecessors warned against it, and they feared to reach it despite the abundance of their knowledge and the strength of their religion; and here we have reached it with the intensity of our weakness in knowledge and religion – God is the One whose help is sought. And God.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage is a social and religious critique of the author's own era. It begins by concluding a previous argument: 'And by this you know that this refuge in our time is not secure' – referring to the refuge of taqlid (imitative faith) that was earlier mentioned as a possible comfort for Fakhr al-Din. The author argues that even taqlid is no longer safe because there is no proper execution of beliefs even by imitation, due to complete neglect of teaching the beliefs of the religion, especially for women and children. Slave-women and slaves are not taught at all, as if they are animals without religious obligations. He then escalates: ignorance is found even among those who claim knowledge, so how much worse are common folk, women, children, and slaves? Bedouins and those far from any knowledge are beyond description. He describes the minds of most people as rigid, hard to teach, and if they learn anything, they misuse it for worldly gain or arrogance. Only a few are protected by God. Finally, he identifies this time as the very one prophesied in hadiths and feared by the righteous predecessors, yet we have reached it with extreme weakness. The passage is a lament for the state of religious education and a call for reform, though it ends with a prayer for help from God.

Key Terms
حرز
تقليد
عقائد
جهل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author is rejecting taqlid as an invalid form of faith.
Correction: The author actually assumes taqlid could be a refuge, but laments that even taqlid is no longer possible because the basics of belief are not being taught.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say that 'this refuge' (taqlid) is not secure in his time?
A) Because religious scholars have forbidden taqlid.
B) Because people do not properly learn the beliefs even by imitation due to neglect of teaching.
C) Because the rulers oppress anyone who practices taqlid.
D) Because the common folk have rejected religion entirely.

Key Takeaway
In this era, the lack of even basic religious teaching has made taqlid—the last refuge for the weak—untenable, fulfilling the warnings of the righteous predecessors.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the author says that even those who claim knowledge are ignorant of many beliefs, what is the state of your own heart concerning the core beliefs of Islam? Do you have mastery, or just imitation? Will you today identify one belief you only know by taqlid and seek to learn its evidence?
?
Consider the hierarchy: common folk, women, children, slaves, bedouins. If you have been given knowledge, do you actively teach those under your care, or are you part of the neglect? What will you change this week to ensure at least one person around you is taught a fundamental belief?
?
The author describes minds that are rigid, distracted, quick to forget, and that misuse knowledge for worldly gain. Do you see any of these traits in yourself? What one step will you take today to break that pattern—whether by sincere learning, acting on knowledge, or teaching others—so that you are not among those who make religion a ladder for this world?

Umar's Certainty: A Proof of Profound Knowledge
Sanusi cites Umar's story as proof of his profound certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Umar's response to the grave trial exemplifies the highest level of certainty (yaqin) in Allah and why Sanusi uses this as proof of his profound knowledge.

Core Idea
Umar's certainty in Allah made him fearless even before angels.

This passage from Sanusi presents a powerful historical proof of the strength of Umar's faith. When told about the grave trial and the angels, Umar did not waver but asked only if his intellect would remain. The Prophet's affirmation 'Umar is certain' becomes the key evidence for Sanusi's argument that true knowledge of Allah eliminates all fear.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi uses a rhetorical question to begin.
2
Identify the key elements
Find the trial of the grave, Umar's question about his intellect, the Prophet's response, and the angels' knowledge level. Note the phrases 'ilm al-yaqin' and 'ayn al-yaqin'.
These terms indicate degrees of certainty.
3
Understand the author's argument
Sanusi argues that Umar's confidence came from his certainty in Allah, not arrogance. The Prophet's testimony confirms this.
The author quotes Quran 53:3 to affirm the Prophet's truthfulness.
4
Reflect on the meaning of 'muqin'
Consider what it means to be 'certain' (muqin) and how this certainty transforms a person's relationship with the unseen and with fear.
This connects to the broader topic of yaqin in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فكيف يكون ذهنه لمعرفةِ مَنِ الكائنات كلُّها مُطبقةٌ على واضح الدلالةِ عليه جل وعزَّ ؟! وانظر قولَهُ رضي الله عنهُ لمَّا أخبره النبيُّ صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ بفتنةِ القبرِ وسؤال الملكين وصفتهما ، فقال : أيكونُ معي عقلي فقالَ : « نَعَمْ نَعَمْ » ، فقال : إذاً أكفيكَهما ، فقال النبيُّ صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ : ( إِنَّ عُمَرَ لَمُوقِنٌ ). فانظر إلى وثوقِهِ رضيَ اللهُ عنهُ بنظرِ عقلِهِ وعدم اكتراثه بمناظرةِ مَنْ علمه مترقٌ عن علم اليقين إلى عين اليقين ؛ وهم الملائكةُ ، ولم يخف أن يشغل فكرَهُ هولُ منظرهما ، ولا فظاعة القبرِ الذي هو أوَّلُ منزلٍ مِنْ منازل الآخرة ، وهل تصدرُ هذه المقالة إلا ممَّنْ مُزِجَتْ معرفة الله تعالى بلحمه ودمه حتى تلاشى عندَهُ كلُّ ما سواه ، ولم يخف غيره ؟! وانظرْ قول النبيِّ صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ : « إِنَّ عُمَرَ لَمُوقِنٌ )، وهو الصادق المصدوقُ ، وَمَا يَنطِقُ عَنِ الْهَوَى [ النجم : ٣]
How can his mind be for knowing the One upon whom all beings are agreed with clear indication, glorified and exalted?! And look at his saying - may Allah be pleased with him - when the Prophet ﷺ informed him of the trial of the grave and the questioning of the two angels and their description, he said: 'Will my intellect be with me?' He said: 'Yes, yes.' He said: 'Then I suffice against them.' The Prophet ﷺ said: 'Indeed, Umar is certain.' So look at his confidence - may Allah be pleased with him - in the view of his intellect and his lack of concern for debating those whose knowledge had progressed from knowledge of certainty (ilm al-yaqin) to the eye of certainty (ayn al-yaqin), and they are the angels. And he did not fear that the horror of their sight would occupy his mind, nor the terror of the grave which is the first station among the stations of the Hereafter. And does this statement come except from one whose knowledge of Allah the Exalted has been mixed with his flesh and blood, such that everything other than Him has vanished in his sight, and he does not fear anyone else? And look at the saying of the Prophet ﷺ: 'Indeed, Umar is certain,' and he is the truthful and believed, and he does not speak from desire (Quran 53:3).
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage is a proof by example demonstrating the extreme certainty (yaqin) of Umar ibn al-Khattab. The author begins with a rhetorical question highlighting that the entire cosmos points clearly to its Creator. He then recounts the story of Umar's response to the Prophet's description of the grave trial: Umar asks if his intellect will be with him, and upon affirmation, declares he needs no other weapon. The Prophet's confirmation 'Umar is certain' is then cited. The author comments on Umar's confidence in his intellect and his lack of fear even of angels whose knowledge surpasses ordinary certainty, reaching the level of 'ayn al-yaqin - seeing with direct vision. This demonstrates that Umar's knowledge had permeated his entire being, eliminating all fear of anything besides Allah. The passage concludes by quoting the Quranic verse that the Prophet does not speak from desire, reinforcing the veracity of the hadith.

Key Terms
يقين
علم اليقين
عين اليقين
عقل
ملك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Umar's confidence was arrogance or overconfidence.
Correction: Umar's confidence was due to his profound certainty in Allah, not arrogance. The Prophet himself confirmed his state as 'muqin' (certain).

Checkpoint
What did Umar say when told about the trial of the grave and the two angels?
A) I am very afraid.
B) Will my intellect be with me?
C) I will flee to the desert.
D) Do not worry about me.

Key Takeaway
Umar's certainty in Allah allowed him to face the grave trial with confidence, relying on his intellect illuminated by faith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you contemplate the trials of the grave and the questioning angels, do you feel the same unshakable confidence as Umar? What would it take for you to genuinely say, without hesitation, 'I suffice against them'?
?
Umar's intellect was so steeped in knowledge of Allah that fear of everything else vanished. Is your intellect mixed with the same certainty, or does it waver when facing the unknown? What must you learn or unlearn today to make that true?
?
If the Prophet ﷺ were to personally tell you about a future trial you will face, would you trust your intellect and Allah's promise enough to remain calm, or would panic and doubt overtake you? What change in your heart is needed now to prepare for that moment?
