Al-Qadi on Faith and Knowledge
Sanusi explains and defends Al-Qadi's position on knowledge as a condition for true faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Qadi's position that true faith (iman) requires knowledge (ma'rifa) and how logical analysis shows that imitators (muqallidun) are not true believers in God's judgment.

Core Idea
True faith is impossible without direct knowledge; imitation falls short of real belief.

This passage dives into a heated debate about the nature of faith. Al-Qadi argues that genuine faith must be based on knowledge, not mere imitation. Sanusi explains the logical structure of Al-Qadi's claim, using tools from rhetoric and logic to clarify that only those who truly know God are believers in His sight.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation to get the flow of the argument.
Notice how Sanusi begins by refuting a transmitted view before presenting Al-Qadi's position.
2
Identify Al-Qadi's principle
Find where Sanusi states that faith requires knowledge, not taqlid. Understand the definition of faith as 'assent that follows knowledge'.
Al-Qadi distinguishes true faith from mere traditional belief or opinion.
3
Examine the logical analysis
Sanusi analyzes Al-Qadi's statement using rhetoric (qasr ifrad) and logic (universal affirmative proposition and its contrapositive). Trace how 'Every believer is knowledgeable' leads to 'No imitator is a believer'.
The contrapositive (inverse of the congruent contradictory) is key.
4
Understand the judgment distinction
Notice that Al-Qadi's judgment is about God's view based on inner reality, not our external judgment. Also note that knowledge is in the heart, and verbal expression is not required.
This clarifies that the dispute is about whether those we call believers externally actually possess knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونُقِلَ عن طائفة مِنْ أهل العلم أنَّ الله معروف بضرورة العقل، وأنَّهُ غرزَ معرفةَ وجودِهِ في خلقِهِ ، وما أُقيمَ مِنَ الأَدلَّةِ على ذلكَ إِنَّما استدلال على أنواع الضرورة ، وظاهر هذا عين ما أنكرتَ. هو قلتُ : ليس عينه ، ولا يدلُّ عليه . أما قولُ القاضي : فهو جارٍ على أصلِهِ وأصل الجمهورِ مِنْ أَنَّ التقليد لا تحصلُ معَهُ حقيقة الإيمانِ ، وإنَّما تحصل مع المعرفة ، ولهذا كانت حقيقة الإيمانِ عندَ القاضي هو التصديق التابع للمعرفة ، واحترز بقوله : ( التابع للمعرفة ) مِنَ التصديق التابع للاعتقادِ التقليدي أو التابع للظنّ أو الشكّ أو الوهم ، فمعنى قوله : ( لا يُوجَدُ مؤمن إلا وهو عارف باللهِ تعالى ) لا يُوجَدُ مؤمن شرعاً ـ أي : في حكم الله تعالى المبني على التحقيقِ وما في نفس الأمر ، لا في حكمنا نحنُ المبني على الظواهر - إلا وهو عارفُ ؛ أي : فمَنْ ليس بعارف ؛ كالمقلد ونحوه .. فليس بمؤمن عندَ اللهِ سبحانه فالقصر في لفظه قصرُ إفراد ؛ ردّاً على مَنْ يتوهَّمُ اشتراك العارفِ والمقلد مثلاً في صدقِ حقيقة الإيمانِ ، فنيَّة بقصرِ المؤمن على العارفِ : على خروج غيرِ العارفِ مِنْ حقيقة الإيمان ، هذا إن نظرنا في اللفظ بطريق فنّ البلاغةِ وإن نظرنا فيه بطريقِ فنّ المنطقِ ، فهو في قُوَّةِ قضية كليَّةٍ موجبة قائلة : كلُّ مؤمن فهو عارفٌ ، وهذه القضية يلزمها بعكس النقيض الموافق : كلُّ مَنْ ليس بعارفِ فليس بمؤمن ، وبعكس النقيض المخالفِ : لا شيء مِنْ غيرِ العارفِ بمؤمِنٍ ، فجعله كبرى لقضية صادقة ؛ وهي قولنا : كلُّ مُقلّد فهو غيرُ عارف .. ينتجُ مِنَ الأَوَّلِ : لا شيء مِنَ المقلد بمؤمن ، وأحرى مَنْ كَانَتْ درجتُهُ دونَ درجةِ التقليدِ الصحيح ، كما هو حال كثيرٍ ممَّنْ ينطق بكلمتي الإيمان . وأمَّا قولُ القاضي : ( فمنهم قوي القريحة ... ) إلى آخره : فبين ؛ لأنَّ المعرفة محلها القلبُ ، وسببها العادي ـ وهو النظر ـ عقلي أيضاً، والنطقُ باللسان لا أثر لهُ فيهما ، فلهذا لم يكن شرطاً فيها ، بل المقصود حصول العقائدِ في القلب بأدلَّتِها المنتجة لها عقلاً ، قَدَرَ أَنْ يُعبّر عن ذلكَ مَنْ حصلَتْ له أم لا ، ولا ريب في حصولِ حقيقة الإيمان لمثل هذا ، وليسَ نزاعُنا فيهِ ، وإِنَّما نزاعُنا في المعرفة : هل يقولُ القاضي : إنَّها حاصلةٌ لكلِّ مَنْ نطلق عليهِ نحنُ اسم الإيمان بناءً على الظاهر أم لا ؟
And it was reported from a group of scholars that Allah is known by necessity of reason, and that He implanted the knowledge of His existence in His creation, and what proofs are established for that are only inferences based on types of necessity, and the apparent meaning of this is exactly what you denied! He said: I said: It is not exactly that, nor does it imply it. As for the saying of Al-Qadi: it proceeds from his principle and the principle of the majority that imitation (taqlid) does not produce the reality of faith, but rather it is produced with knowledge, and for this reason the reality of faith according to Al-Qadi is assent (tasdiq) that follows knowledge, and he restricted by his saying 'that follows knowledge' from assent that follows traditional belief or that follows opinion or doubt or illusion. So the meaning of his saying: 'There is no believer except that he is knowledgeable of Allah' is: there is no believer according to the Law – i.e., in the judgment of Allah based on verification and what is in reality, not in our judgment based on appearances – except that he is knowledgeable; i.e., whoever is not knowledgeable, like the imitator and the like, is not a believer in the sight of Allah. So the restriction in his wording is a restriction of isolation (qasr ifrad) as a refutation of those who imagine that the knowledgeable person and the imitator share in the truth of faith. So the intent of restricting the believer to the knowledgeable is to exclude the non-knowledgeable from the reality of faith. This is if we consider the wording by the method of rhetoric; and if we consider it by the method of logic, it is in the strength of a universal affirmative proposition saying: Every believer is knowledgeable. This proposition entails by the inverse of the congruent contradictory (aks al-naqid al-muwafiq): Everyone who is not knowledgeable is not a believer; and by the inverse of the opposite contradictory (aks al-naqid al-mukhalif): None of the non-knowledgeable is a believer. So if we make it the major premise of a true proposition, which is our saying: Every imitator is non-knowledgeable, it produces from the first: None of the imitators is a believer, and more so for those whose rank is below that of correct imitation, as is the case with many of those who utter the two words of faith. As for the saying of Al-Qadi: 'Among them are those of strong intellect...' etc., it is clear, because knowledge (ma'rifa) has its seat in the heart, and its customary cause – which is reflection (nazar) – is also rational, and speech with the tongue has no effect in them, so for this reason it is not a condition in it. Rather, the intent is the acquisition of beliefs in the heart by their proofs that produce them rationally, whether the one who attains it is able to express it or not. There is no doubt about the attainment of the reality of faith for such a person, and our dispute is not about that; rather our dispute is about knowledge: does Al-Qadi say that it is attained for everyone to whom we apply the name of faith based on appearances or not?
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage is a detailed explanation and defense of Al-Qadi's position on the necessity of knowledge (ma'rifa) for true faith (iman). The author begins by referencing a transmitted view that Allah is known by rational necessity, but he denies that this is the same as what he is arguing. He then explains Al-Qadi's position: faith requires knowledge, and simple imitation (taqlid) does not produce true faith. He clarifies that Al-Qadi's statement 'There is no believer except he is knowledgeable of Allah' is a legal judgment based on reality, not our external judgment. The restriction is a 'qasr ifrad' (restriction of isolation), meaning it isolates the attribute of knowledge to believers, denying it to others. He then provides a logical analysis: the statement is equivalent to a universal affirmative proposition 'Every believer is knowledgeable.' From this, by the rule of contrapositive (inverse of the congruent contradictory), we get 'Everyone who is not knowledgeable is not a believer.' Thus, imitators are excluded from faith. He also addresses Al-Qadi's remark about 'strong intellect' to emphasize that knowledge (ma'rifa) is in the heart, and verbal expression is not a condition. The dispute is whether, in Al-Qadi's view, all those we call believers (by appearance) actually possess this knowledge.

Misconception: Some think Al-Qadi's statement is about how we judge others (based on external profession of faith).
Correction: Al-Qadi's judgment is God's judgment based on inner reality (what is in the heart), not our external judgment based on appearances.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Al-Qadi's assertion that 'there is no believer except he is knowledgeable' logically excludes all imitators from true faith in God's judgment.

Logical Proof That the Imitator Is Not a True Believer
Sanusi uses logical conversion to prove that the muqallid cannot be a true believer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi uses a logical syllogism to demonstrate that a mere imitator (muqallid) cannot be considered a true believer.

Core Idea
Imitative faith without knowledge is logically excluded from true belief.

Sanusi presents a rigorous logical argument to show that simply imitating faith without personal knowledge does not qualify as true belief. By analyzing the statement 'Every believer is a knower,' he derives its logical opposites and constructs a syllogism that excludes the imitator from faith. This passage demonstrates the importance of rational understanding in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the passage carefully, noting the logical terms used by Sanusi.
The passage uses both rhetorical and logical analysis.
2
Identify the key proposition
Find the universal affirmative proposition 'Every believer is a knower' and its two conversions.
The conversions are called 'aks al-naqid'.
3
Follow the syllogism
See how the two premises lead to the conclusion that no imitator is a believer.
The minor premise is 'Every imitator is not a knower'.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why even those below correct imitation are also excluded from faith.
This shows the necessity of knowledge for true faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمقلد مثلاً في صدقِ حقيقة الإيمانِ ، فنيَّة بقضرِ المؤمن على العارفِ : على خروج غيرِ العارفِ مِنْ حقيقة الإيمان ، هذا إن نظرنا في اللفظ بطريق فنّ البلاغةِ وإن نظرنا فيه بطريقِ فنّ المنطقِ ، فهو في قُوَّةِ قضية كليَّةٍ موجبة قائلة : كلُّ مؤمن فهو عارفٌ ، وهذه القضية يلزمها بعكس النقيض الموافق : كلُّ مَنْ ليس بعارفِ فليس بمؤمن ، وبعكس النقيض المخالفِ : لا شيء مِنْ غيرِ العارفِ بمؤمِنٍ ، فجعله كبرى لقضية صادقة ؛ وهي قولنا : كلُّ مُقلّد فهو غيرُ عارف .. ينتجُ مِنَ الأَوَّلِ : لا شيء مِنَ المقلد بمؤمن ، وأحرى مَنْ كَانَتْ درجتُهُ دونَ درجةِ التقليدِ الصحيح ، كما هو حال كثيرٍ ممَّنْ ينطق بكلمتي الإيمان .
And the imitator (muqallid), for example, in the truth of the reality of faith (iman), [the statement] restricting the believer to the knower (arif) implies the exclusion of the non-knower from the reality of faith. This is if we consider the expression according to the art of rhetoric (balagha). But if we consider it according to the art of logic (mantiq), it has the force of an affirmative universal proposition (qadiyya kulliyya) saying: Every believer is a knower. This proposition entails, by conversion of the equivalent opposite ('aks al-naqid al-muwafiq): Everyone who is not a knower is not a believer; and by conversion of the contradictory opposite ('aks al-naqid al-mukhalif): Nothing that is not a knower is a believer. So if we make it a major premise for a true proposition, namely our statement: Every imitator is not a knower, it results from the first [syllogism]: Nothing that is an imitator is a believer, and more so for one whose degree is below the degree of correct imitation (taqlid sahih), as is the case of many who utter the two words of faith.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage is a logical proof that a mere imitator (muqallid) cannot be considered a true believer. The author begins by noting that if we restrict the term 'believer' to the 'knower' (arif), then the non-knower is excluded. He then analyzes this statement rhetorically and logically. Logically, the statement 'Every believer is a knower' is a universal affirmative proposition. It yields two conversions: the equivalent opposite ('aks al-naqid al-muwafiq) gives 'Everyone who is not a knower is not a believer', and the contradictory opposite ('aks al-naqid al-mukhalif) gives 'Nothing that is not a knower is a believer'. Taking 'Every imitator is not a knower' as a second premise, the syllogism concludes that no imitator is a believer. The argument then extends this to those below proper imitation, i.e., those who merely pronounce the shahada without understanding, showing they are also excluded.

Key Terms
مقلد (muqallid)
عارف (arif)
قضية كلية (qadiyya kulliyya)
عكس النقيض (aks al-naqid)
تقليد (taqlid)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The logical argument is just a rhetorical device, not a proof.
Correction: Sanusi uses formal logic to demonstrate that imitative faith cannot be true belief; it is a rigorous proof.

Checkpoint

B) No imitator is a believer.
C) All imitators are believers.
D) The argument is invalid.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi proves that true faith requires knowledge (ma'rifa), and mere imitation without understanding does not qualify as belief.

Knowledge Is of the Heart, Not the Tongue
Sanusi clarifies the Qadi's statement on knowledge and the true point of dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why knowledge (ma'rifa) is an internal state independent of verbal expression, and what the true point of dispute is regarding al-Qadi's position.

Core Idea
Knowledge resides in the heart; speech does not define it.

Sanusi continues his discussion of faith by examining a statement from al-Qadi. He clarifies that knowledge (ma'rifa) is fundamentally an internal reality, seated in the heart and produced by intellectual reflection, not by the tongue. This sets the stage for the actual disagreement about whether such knowledge is present in everyone we call a believer.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice how Sanusi begins by referencing al-Qadi's statement and then explains its clarity.
2
Identify the components of knowledge
Identify where knowledge resides (heart), what causes it (reflection), and why verbal utterance is irrelevant. These are clearly stated.
The footnote corrects the pronoun in the Arabic.
3
Distinguish the two issues
Distinguish between the agreed-upon point (knowledge is internal) and the actual dispute (does al-Qadi say knowledge is present in every outward believer?).
This is the key takeaway.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمَّا قولُ القاضي : ( فمنهم قوي القريحة ... ) إلى آخره : فبين ؛ لأنَّ المعرفة محلها القلبُ ، وسببها العادي ـ وهو النظر ـ عقلي أيضاً، والنطقُ باللسان لا أثر لهُ فيهما ، فلهذا لم يكن شرطاً فيها(١) ، بل المقصود حصول العقائدِ في القلب بأدلَّتِها المنتجة لها عقلاً ، قَدَرَ أَنْ يُعبّر عن ذلكَ مَنْ حصلَتْ له أم لا ، ولا ريب في حصولِ أنَّ حقيقة الإيمان لمثل هذا ، وليسَ نزاعُنا فيهِ ، وإِنَّما نزاعُنا في المعرفة : هل يقولُ القاضي : إنَّها حاصلةٌ لكلِّ مَنْ نطلق عليهِ نحنُ اسم الإيمان بناءً على الظاهر أم لا ؟ (١) الأولى : ( فيهما ) يعني : المعرفة والنظر . مفاد « حامدي » ( ص ۹۲ ) .
As for the statement of al-Qadi: 'Among them are those of strong acumen...' etc., it is clear; because knowledge (ma'rifa) has its seat in the heart, and its ordinary cause—which is reflection (nazar)—is also intellectual, and verbal utterance has no effect on them. For this reason it is not a condition in it. [Footnote: The correct reading is 'in them' meaning knowledge and reflection. According to Hamidi, p. 92.] Rather, the purpose is the acquisition of beliefs in the heart through their proofs that produce them intellectually, whether one is able to express that or not. There is no doubt that the reality of faith occurs for such a person, and our dispute is not about that. Our dispute is about knowledge: does al-Qadi say that it is realized for everyone to whom we apply the name of faith based on outward appearance, or not?
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage discusses and defends the view of al-Qadi (likely al-Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar or another figure) regarding the nature of knowledge (ma'rifa). The author explains that the Qadi's statement is clear: knowledge resides in the heart, its ordinary cause is intellectual reflection (nazar), and verbal expression has no impact on it. Thus, speaking the words of faith is not a condition for true knowledge. The real purpose is that the beliefs (aqa'id) become established in the heart through their rational proofs, regardless of whether the person can articulate them. The author then clarifies that this is not the point of contention; rather, the dispute is about whether al-Qadi holds that such knowledge is present in everyone we outwardly call a believer. The footnote corrects the pronoun to refer to both knowledge and reflection. The passage ultimately distinguishes between the actual possession of ma'rifa and the outward attribution of iman.

Key Terms
القاضي (al-Qadi)
المعرفة (ma'rifa)
النظر (nazar)
القلب (qalb)
النطق (nutq)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowledge (ma'rifa) requires verbal declaration.
Correction: The passage clarifies that knowledge resides in the heart and its cause is intellectual reflection; verbal utterance has no effect on it, so it is not a condition.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is verbal utterance not a condition for knowledge (ma'rifa)?
A) Because verbal utterance is always unreliable.
B) Because knowledge's seat is the heart and its cause is intellectual reflection.
C) Because the Qadi said so without evidence.
D) Because verbal utterance is forbidden.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge (ma'rifa) is an internal state of the heart, produced by reflection, and does not depend on verbal expression; the real dispute is whether such knowledge is present in all outwardly called believers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever considered that your knowledge of Allah might be merely on your tongue, not in your heart? How often do you find yourself repeating phrases of faith without the corresponding inner conviction? This lesson shows that true knowledge is in the heart, produced by reflection. Take a moment to reflect: is your faith rooted in deep personal reflection on the proofs, or is it just inherited words? What is one proof you can meditate upon today to strengthen your inner ma'rifa?
?
If verbal expression is not required for knowledge, how does that challenge your assumption that people who cannot articulate their faith might still be true believers? How might this affect your judgment of others, especially those less articulate? Will you change how you perceive and interact with them?
?
The lesson says the purpose is acquisition of beliefs in the heart through their intellectual proofs. Are you actively seeking to acquire these proofs, or are you relying on hearsay? What steps will you take to engage in nazar (reflection) on the evidences of faith this week? Write down one specific proof and commit to reflecting on it until it settles in your heart.

Certainty About Inner Faith is Impossible
Sanusi argues against certainty about inner faith, citing a hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why we cannot be certain of another person's inner faith and why we must judge only by outward appearances.

Core Idea
Inner faith is known only to Allah; we judge only by outward Islam.

Sanusi addresses a key methodological principle: while we affirm that faith is in the heart, we cannot claim certainty about anyone's inner state. This lesson examines the rational and scriptural basis for this limitation, using a famous hadith.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then the full translation. Notice the flow from rational argument to hadith citation.
The passage has two clear sections: a rational argument and a scriptural proof.
2
Identify the rational argument
Sanusi lists possibilities for a person who outwardly shows faith: they could be an imitator, a conjecturer, a doubter, a deluder, or even a hidden heretic. Even eloquent proofs do not guarantee inner certainty.
The key point is that we can only have preponderant conjecture (zann), not certainty (qat').
3
Understand the hadith proof
The Prophet rebuked Sa'd for saying 'I consider him a believer' and told him 'Or a Muslim,' directing him to judge by the outward appearance of Islam, not inner faith.
This hadith establishes that inner faith (iman) is part of the hidden (batin) known only to Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى القطع أنَّ هذا ممَّا لا يقوله القاضي ولا غيرُه ، بل كلُّ عاقل يُجوِّزُ فيمَنْ يُظهرُ الإيمانَ أنْ يكونَ فيهِ مُقلّداً أو ظاناً أو شاكاً أو مُتوهّماً ، بل ويُجوِّزُ أنْ يكونَ كافراً زنديقاً ، بل لو نطقَ مُظهِرُ الإيمانِ بأدلَّتِهِ وأتقن براهينَهُ.. لَمَا قطعنا في حقه بالإيمان ولا المعرفة ؛ لاحتمالِ أنْ يكونَ في قلبه شبهات أو جبَتْ له شكاً ولم يبدِها لنا ، أو حفظ تلك الأدلة تقليداً ولم يتحققها ، إلا أن قرائن الأحوالِ تُغلب الظن بأحد الأمرين . وبالجملة : فالإيمانُ لمَّا كانَ مرجعه إلى المعرفة ، والمعرفةُ مِنَ السرائر ، والله سبحانَهُ متولّيها .. فلا تُعرَفُ إِلا مِنْ قِبَلِهِ ، ولهذا زجر النبيُّ صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ سعداً رضي الله عنه عن جَزْمِهِ بالإيمانِ في حق الرجل الذي أمسك النبيُّ صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ عن عطائِهِ ، فقالَ لهُ سعد : ما لك عن فلان ؟! فواللهِ ؛ إِنِّي لَأَراه مؤمناً ! - بفتح همزة ( أراه ) أي : أعلمُهُ - فقالَ صَلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ : « أَوْ مُسْلِماً » ، بإسكان الواو ، على الإضراب عن قوله إلى الحكم بالظاهرِ ، كأنَّه قالَ : بل تراه مسلماً ، فما بالك تقطعُ بإيمانِهِ ؟! لأَنَّهُ مِنَ الباطن الذي لا يعلمُهُ إلا اللهُ عزَّ وجلَّ ، والحديث خرَّجَهُ البخاري ومسلم وغيرهما (۱) (۱) صحيح البخاري ( ۲۷ ) ، صحیح مسلم ( ١٥٠ ) عن سيدنا سعد بن أبي وقاص رضي الله عنه .
And it is certain that this is something that neither al-Qadi nor anyone else says. Rather, every rational person allows, concerning one who outwardly manifests faith, that he may be an imitator (muqallid), or a conjecturer (zann), or a doubter (shakk), or a deluder (mutawahhim); nay, he allows that he may be a disbelieving heretic (kafir zindiq). Even if the one manifesting faith utters its proofs and perfects its demonstrations, we still do not have certainty (qat') about his faith or knowledge; because of the possibility that there are doubts (shubhat) in his heart, or that he has contracted skepticism without revealing it to us, or that he memorized those proofs by imitation without realizing them. However, the indications of circumstances (qara'in al-ahwal) may preponderate conjecture (zann) toward one of the two possibilities. In summary: since faith's reference is to knowledge, and knowledge is among the secrets (sara'ir), and Allah, exalted is He, is its guardian, it is not known except by Him. For this reason, the Prophet (peace and blessings be upon him) rebuked Sa'd (may Allah be pleased with him) for his categorical assertion of faith concerning a man from whom the Prophet withheld his gift. Sa'd said to him: 'What is the matter with you concerning so-and-so? By Allah, I consider him a believer!'—with the hamza of 'arahu' opened, meaning 'I know him'—so the Prophet (peace and blessings be upon him) said: 'Or a Muslim' (aw musliman), with the waw made silent, indicating a turning away from his statement to the judgment based on outward appearance, as if he said: 'Rather, you consider him a Muslim. So why do you affirm his faith?!' Because it is of the inner hidden (batin) which none knows except Allah Almighty. The hadith was recorded by al-Bukhari, Muslim, and others. (1) Sahih al-Bukhari (27), Sahih Muslim (150) from our master Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas, may Allah be pleased with him.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage argues for the impossibility of certainty (qat') regarding another person's inner faith. The author begins by stating that no rational person claims certainty about the inner state of one who outwardly shows faith. Such a person could be a mere imitator, a conjecturer, a doubter, a deluder, or even a hidden heretic. Even if he eloquently states proofs, we cannot be certain because he might hide doubts or simply repeat arguments by rote. The best we can have is a preponderant conjecture (zann) based on external indications. The root principle is that faith returns to knowledge (ma'rifa), which is part of the hidden secrets (sara'ir) known only to Allah. As proof, the author cites the hadith of Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas, where the Prophet corrected Sa'd for saying 'I consider him a believer' to 'or a Muslim,' indicating that we should only affirm outward Islam, not inner faith. The hadith emphasizes that inner belief is something only Allah knows. The passage concludes with the hadith's authenticity.

Key Terms
قطع (qat')
ظن (zann)
قرائن الأحوال (qara'in al-ahwal)
سرائر (sara'ir)
باطن (batin)
مسلم (muslim)
مؤمن (mu'min)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One can be certain of another's inner faith if they articulate proofs well.
Correction: Even eloquent proofs do not guarantee inner faith; the person may be imitating or hiding doubts. Only Allah knows the heart.

Checkpoint
What did the Prophet teach Sa'd by saying 'Or a Muslim' (aw musliman) instead of accepting Sa'd's statement 'I consider him a believer'?
A) That the man was actually a Muslim, not a believer.
B) That we should only affirm outward Islam, not inner faith.
C) That Sa'd was wrong and the man was a disbeliever.
D) That the man was a hypocrite.

Key Takeaway
We must not claim certainty about anyone's inner faith; only Allah knows the heart; we judge only by outward Islam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a fellow Muslim making a mistake in religious practice, do you immediately assume they have weak faith? How does this lesson force you to reconsider that assumption and what will you change in your heart?
?
Have you ever felt certain about someone else's inner state—whether their hypocrisy or their piety—and spoken about it? Knowing now that only Allah knows the heart, what concrete step will you take to stop judging what you cannot know?
?
Can you honestly admit that your own faith might contain elements of imitation, doubt, or hidden shubhat? How does this passage challenge you to move beyond mere outward conformity and seek true knowledge from your heart?

The Principle: Only Divine Revelation Knows Inner Faith
Sanusi states the principle and provides rational argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why human certainty about another's inner faith is impossible and what rational possibilities Sanusi lists to demonstrate this.

Core Idea
Outward faith can hide inner doubt, imitation, or even disbelief.

Sanusi opens with a bold claim: no one can definitively judge another's faith except by divine revelation. He then grounds this in rational possibilities—the outwardly faithful may secretly be in doubt, following blindly, or even a heretic. This lesson unpacks that reasoning and its implications for how we view others.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the strong opening statement and the list of possibilities.
The passage uses 'بل' multiple times to escalate the argument.
2
Identify the core principle
Find the sentence that states the principle about tawqif. Then note how Sanusi emphasizes that even judges do not claim certainty.
3
List the inner possibilities
Sanusi lists four states a person might be in despite outward faith: muqallid, zann, shakk, mutawahhim. Then he adds possible kufr. Write them down.
This shows the range of hidden realities.
4
Understand the role of circumstantial evidence
The passage mentions qara'in al-ahwal giving preponderant presumption, not certainty. Note the limit: only Allah knows the heart fully.
This is a nuanced point: we can have strong suspicion, not certainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإيمانُ لا يقطعُ أحدٌ به في حق غيرِهِ إلا بتوقيف . وعلى القطع أنَّ هذا ممَّا لا يقوله القاضي ولا غيرُه ، بل كلُّ عاقل يُجوِّزُ فيمَنْ يُظهرُ الإيمانَ أنْ يكونَ فيهِ مُقلّداً أو ظاناً أو شاكاً أو مُتوهّماً ، بل ويُجوِّزُ أنْ يكونَ كافراً زنديقاً ، بل لو نطقَ مُظهِرُ الإيمانِ بأدلَّتِهِ وأتقن براهينَهُ.. لَمَا قطعنا في حقه بالإيمان ولا المعرفة ؛ لاحتمالِ أنْ يكونَ في قلبه شبهات أو جبَتْ له شكاً ولم يبدِها لنا ، أو حفظ تلك الأدلة تقليداً ولم يتحققها ، إلا أن قرائن الأحوالِ تُغلب الظن بأحد الأمرين . وبالجملة : فالإيمانُ لمَّا كانَ مرجعه إلى المعرفة ، والمعرفةُ مِنَ السرائر ، والله سبحانَهُ متولّيها .. فلا تُعرَفُ إِلا مِنْ قِبَلِهِ ، ولهذا زجر النبيُّ صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ سعداً رضي الله عنه عن جَزْمِهِ بالإيمانِ في حق الرجل الذي أمسك النبيُّ صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ عن عطائِهِ ، فقالَ لهُ سعد : ما لك عن فلان ؟! فواللهِ ؛ إِنِّي لَأَراه مؤمناً ! - بفتح همزة ( أراه ) أي : أعلمُهُ - فقالَ صَلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ : « أَوْ مُسْلِماً » ، بإسكان الواو ، على الإضراب عن قوله إلى الحكم بالظاهرِ ، كأنَّه قالَ : بل تراه مسلماً ، فما بالك تقطعُ بإيمانِهِ ؟! لأَنَّهُ مِنَ الباطن الذي لا يعلمُهُ إلا اللهُ عزَّ وجلَّ ، والحديث خرَّجَهُ البخاري ومسلم وغيرهما (١) صحيح البخاري ( ٢٧ ) ، صحیح مسلم ( ١٥٠ ) عن سيدنا سعد بن أبي وقاص رضي الله عنه
Faith—no one can definitively judge it regarding another except by divine revelation. And it is certain that this is something that al-Qadi nor anyone else says; rather, every rational person allows that one who outwardly displays faith may be a muqallid (blind follower), or a zann (one with mere opinion), or a shakk (one in doubt), or a mutawahhim (one with illusion), and even allows that he may be a disbelieving zindiq (heretic). Indeed, even if the one displaying faith utters his proofs and perfects his arguments, we would not definitively judge him to have faith or knowledge, due to the possibility that there may be doubts in his heart that have taken hold of him as doubt and he did not disclose them to us, or that he memorized those proofs by imitation and did not verify them. Except that circumstantial evidence (qara'in al-ahwal) may make one of the two matters preponderant as presumption. In short: since faith's reference is to knowledge (ma'rifa), and knowledge is among the innermost secrets (sara'ir), and Allah—glorified is He—is the one who oversees it, it is not known except from Him. For this reason, the Prophet (peace be upon him) rebuked Sa'd (may Allah be pleased with him) for his definitive assertion of faith regarding the man from whom the Prophet (peace be upon him) withheld his gift. Sa'd said to him: 'What is the matter with you regarding so-and-so? By Allah, I indeed see him as a believer!'—with fatha on the hamza of 'arahu, meaning 'I know him'—so the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Or a Muslim?' with sukun on the waw, indicating a shift from his statement to the apparent judgment, as if he said: 'Rather, you see him as a Muslim, so why do you definitively assert his faith?' Because it is part of the inner (batin) which none knows except Allah Almighty. The hadith is recorded by al-Bukhari and Muslim and others. (1) Sahih al-Bukhari (27), Sahih Muslim (150) from our master Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas (may Allah be pleased with him).
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage is a proof and explanation of the principle that no one can definitively judge another person's true faith (iman) except by divine revelation (tawqif). Sanusi begins by stating this principle outright. He then clarifies that even the judge (al-Qadi, likely Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar) does not claim otherwise; every rational person acknowledges that someone who outwardly displays faith may in reality be a blind follower (muqallid), a person of mere opinion (zann), doubt (shakk), or illusion (tawahhum), or even a hidden disbeliever (zindiq). Sanusi strengthens the point: even if a person articulates theological proofs eloquently, we still cannot be certain of his inner belief, because he might harbor hidden doubts, or have memorized the proofs without genuine verification. The only exception is when circumstantial evidence (qara'in al-ahwal) makes one possibility preponderant as a presumption (ghalabat al-zann), but not certainty. The underlying reason is that faith is rooted in inner knowledge (ma'rifa), which is one of the innermost secrets (sara'ir) that only Allah knows. To support this, Sanusi cites the hadith of Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas. When the Prophet (peace be upon him) withheld a gift from a certain man, Sa'd assumed the man was a believer because of his apparent actions. The Prophet corrected him, saying 'Or a Muslim?' meaning Sa'd should only judge based on outward appearance (zahir) and not assert inner faith (iman). The hadith demonstrates that the inner state is known only to Allah.

Key Terms
إيمان
توقيف
تقليد
ظن
شك
توهم
قرائن الأحوال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One can be certain of another's true faith based on their outward practice or articulate arguments.
Correction: Sanusi argues that certainty is impossible because inner states (doubt, imitation, disbelief) can be hidden even behind eloquent proofs.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, even if someone perfectly articulates proofs of faith, why can we not be certain of his inner belief?
A) Because he might be a liar.
B) Because he might have hidden doubts or have memorized without verification.
C) Because only the Prophet can judge.
D) Because we need multiple witnesses.

Key Takeaway
Human certainty about another's inner faith is impossible; only Allah knows the heart, and circumstantial evidence only yields presumption.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed someone's faith was strong because they spoke eloquently about Islam or debated well? How does this passage challenge that assumption and what does it reveal about your own tendency to judge hearts?
?
When you look at your own heart, do you recognize moments of doubt, imitation, or hidden disbelief that you hide from others? What would it mean to be honest with yourself and Allah about these inner states?
?
This lesson teaches that even perfect articulation of proofs does not guarantee true faith in yourself or others. Are you relying on your own ability to argue or memorize as a proof of your own iman? What would it take to seek genuine verification from your heart?

The Hadith of Sa'd: Judging by the Outer, Not the Inner
Sanusi gives the reason and prophetic proof for the principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the lesson from the Prophet's correction of Sa'd: we can only judge based on outward appearance (zahir), not claim knowledge of inner faith.

Core Idea
The Prophet taught Sa'd to say 'Muslim' not 'believer' regarding another's inner state.

Sanusi concludes his argument with a powerful prophetic example. When Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas declared a man a believer based on apparent actions, the Prophet corrected him: 'Or a Muslim?' This lesson explores that hadith and its distinction between judging by the outer vs. the inner, which only Allah knows.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and hadith
Read the Arabic from 'وبالجملة' to the end. Focus on the hadith narrative and the Prophet's response.
Notice the linguistic note about 'أو' with sukun indicating correction.
2
Identify the underlying reason
Before the hadith, Sanusi explains why faith cannot be known: it is based on inner knowledge (ma'rifa), which is among the secrets (sara'ir) that only Allah oversees.
3
Analyze the Prophet's correction
The Prophet says 'أو مسلماً' (Or a Muslim?) to shift Sa'd from claiming knowledge of inner faith (iman) to judging by outward submission (islam). Note the linguistic shift.
The hadith shows the distinction between islam (outward) and iman (inner).
4
Apply the lesson
Reflect on how this hadith prohibits us from definitively calling anyone a true believer (mu'min) based on what we see. We can only affirm their outward Islam.
This has practical implications for how we treat others.

This passage is a proof and explanation of the principle that no one can definitively judge another person's true faith (iman) except by divine revelation (tawqif). Sanusi begins by stating this principle outright. He then clarifies that even the judge (al-Qadi, likely Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar) does not claim otherwise; every rational person acknowledges that someone who outwardly displays faith may in reality be a blind follower (muqallid), a person of mere opinion (zann), doubt (shakk), or illusion (tawahhum), or even a hidden disbeliever (zindiq). Sanusi strengthens the point: even if a person articulates theological proofs eloquently, we still cannot be certain of his inner belief, because he might harbor hidden doubts, or have memorized the proofs without genuine verification. The only exception is when circumstantial evidence (qara'in al-ahwal) makes one possibility preponderant as a presumption (ghalabat al-zann), but not certainty. The underlying reason is that faith is rooted in inner knowledge (ma'rifa), which is one of the innermost secrets (sara'ir) that only Allah knows. To support this, Sanusi cites the hadith of Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas. When the Prophet (peace be upon him) withheld a gift from a certain man, Sa'd assumed the man was a believer because of his apparent actions. The Prophet corrected him, saying 'Or a Muslim?' meaning Sa'd should only judge based on outward appearance (zahir) and not assert inner faith (iman). The hadith demonstrates that the inner state is known only to Allah.

Key Terms
معرفة
سرائر
ظاهر
باطن
إيمان
إسلام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: We can call someone a true believer (mu'min) based on their actions and statements.
Correction: The Prophet corrected Sa'd, showing we can only affirm outward submission (muslim), not inner faith.

Checkpoint
What did the Prophet say to Sa'd when Sa'd claimed to know a man was a believer?
A) 'You are right, he is a believer.'
B) 'Do not say that; he is a hypocrite.'
C) 'Or a Muslim?' (أو مسلماً)
D) 'Only Allah knows.'

Key Takeaway
Only Allah knows the inner faith; we must limit our judgments to outward appearance, calling others 'Muslim' not 'believer' with certainty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of someone you have silently judged as 'not a true believer' because of a sin you saw. By what right did you peer into their heart? This hadith demands repentance—will you replace that judgment with 'He is a Muslim, Allah knows best'?
?
Your own heart is a secret even to you. Have you ever felt secure in your iman based on your outer worship? What if your inner reality is not what you assume? How will you seek to truly know your own faith?
?
When a fellow Muslim's actions confuse you, do you automatically assume their iman is weak? The Prophet taught Sa'ad to say 'Muslim'—a label that leaves room for Allah's knowledge. Are you willing to give others that same mercy?

Self-Knowledge and the Danger of Imagining Knowledge
Sanusi warns against self-deception and distinguishes taqlid from ma'rifa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the importance of sincere self-knowledge and the difference between true ma'rifa and mere taqlid.

Core Idea
Claiming knowledge without awareness of how you know is not knowledge.

Sanusi shifts from judging others to self-examination. He warns that many people who think they have true knowledge are actually only following blind imitation. This lesson explores the danger of self-deception in matters of faith.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
Sanusi moves from judging others to self-knowledge. A rational person knows his own state, but some ignorant ones mistakenly think they have knowledge.
The passage contrasts the outward judgment of others with inner self-awareness.
2
Read the passage on taqlid and ma'rifa
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Notice how taqlid is described as a disputed degree, and the one who imagines he has knowledge is actually ignorant.
3
Reflect on the imam's saying
The imam says: 'Whoever thinks he knows and does not know how he knows has not known.' This emphasizes that true knowledge requires understanding its basis.
This saying is a key warning against self-deception.
4
Recognize the widespread failure
Sanusi adds that many have not even mastered the creeds to the degree of taqlid. This shows the prevalence of ignorance.
This connects to the next lesson on al-Qadi's definition of kufr.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا كله في حق الغيرِ المظهر للإيمان . وأما الإنسانُ في نفسِهِ : فهو أعرفُ بحالِهِ إِنْ كَانَ عاقلاً ، ومِنَ الجَهَلَةِ مَنْ لم يعرفُ حالَ نفسِهِ ، فهو في درجة التقليد المختلف فيها ، ويَتوهَّمُ أنَّهُ في درجةِ المعرفة ، ولهذا قال بعضُ الأئمةِ : مَنْ ظنَّ أنَّه عرف ولم يدر كيف عرف .. فلم يعرف . ومنهم مَنْ لم يتقن العقائد ولو بدرجةِ التقليد ، وهو كثير ، وهذا الذي حملنا عليه قول القاضي ؛ مِنْ أنَّ مراده بالمؤمن : المؤمنُ عند الله تعالى وفي شرعِهِ ، لا مَنْ نُطلق عليه نحنُ لفظ المؤمن بناءً على الظاهر ، قد صَرَّحَ بمعناه شرفُ الدين بنُ التلمساني في ( شرح المعالم )، حيث تَعرَّضَ لِمَنْ يُحكَمُ عليهِ بالإيمانِ ولَمَنْ يُحكَمُ عليهِ بالكفرِ ، فنقل عن القاضي أنَّ حقيقةَ الإيمان الشرعي ترجع إلى المعرفة والتصديقِ بالقلبِ ، قال : ( فالكفر يرجع إلى الجهل بما شُرِطَ علمه في الإيمان إجماعاً ، أو التكذيب بهِ(۱) ، وكذلك الإعراضُ عنِ النظر في التوحيدِ كفَرٌ ؛ لِمَا يلزمُهُ مِنَ الجهل ، وكذلكَ الشقُّ الظنُّ ؛ فإنَّهما يستلزمانِ انتفاء المعرفة ، والتقليد عند القاضي ومَنْ تابعَهُ مِنَ الجمهور كذلك ((۲) فانظر عزوَهُ كفَرَ المُعرِضِ عن النظر والمقلد إلى القاضي (١) قوله : ( إجماعاً ) راجع لقوله : ( شرط ( ولقوله : ( يرجع ) ، وقوله : ( أو التكذيب به ) أي : بما شرط علمه . . . ، وهو عطف على الجهل . « حامدي » ( ص ٩٤ ) (٢) شرح معالم أصول الدين ( ص ٦٦٠ )
All this is regarding the other who outwardly displays faith. As for a person regarding himself: he is more knowledgeable of his own state if he is rational. And among the ignorant are those who do not know their own state; he is in the degree of blind imitation (taqlid) which is disputed, and he imagines that he is in the degree of knowledge (ma'rifa). For this reason, one of the imams said: 'Whoever thinks that he knows and does not know how he knows has not known.' And among them are those who have not mastered the creeds (aqa'id) even to the degree of imitation (taqlid), and they are many. And this is what led us to accept the saying of al-Qadi: that his intent by 'believer' (mu'min) is 'the believer in the sight of Allah Most High and in His law', not the one to whom we apply the word 'believer' based on the outward appearance (zahir). Sharaf al-Din ibn al-Tilmisani clarified its meaning in 'Sharh al-Ma'alim', where he discussed who is judged to have belief (iman) and who is judged to have disbelief (kufr). He narrated from al-Qadi that the reality of legal faith (iman shar'i) reduces to inner knowledge (ma'rifa) and affirmation (tasdiq) by the heart. He said: 'So kufr reduces to ignorance (jahl) of what knowledge of it is required in faith by consensus, or to denial (takdhib) of it. Likewise, turning away (i'rad) from reflection (nazar) upon tawhid is kufr because of the ignorance it entails. Likewise, doubt (shakk) and mere opinion (zann), for they necessitate the absence of knowledge. And imitation (taqlid) according to al-Qadi and those who followed him from the majority (jumhur) is likewise.' So observe his attribution of the kufr of the one who turns away from reflection (mu'rid) and the imitator (muqallid) to al-Qadi. (1) His saying: 'by consensus' refers to his saying 'condition' and his saying 'reduces', and his saying 'or denial of it' means denial of what knowledge of it is required, etc., and it is a conjunction of al-jahl. 'Hamidi' (p. 94). (2) Sharh Ma'alim Usul al-Din (p. 660).
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage is a continuation of the discussion on faith and judgment. It first addresses self-knowledge: a rational person knows his own state, but some ignorant people mistakenly think they have true knowledge (ma'rifa) when they only have blind imitation (taqlid). Sanusi then notes that this is why he accepts al-Qadi's position that the term 'believer' when used in legal judgments refers to the person who is truly a believer in Allah's sight, not merely the one we label based on outward appearance. He cites Sharaf al-Din ibn al-Tilmisani's commentary, which elaborates al-Qadi's view. According to al-Qadi, the reality of legal faith (iman shar'i) is inner knowledge (ma'rifa) and affirmation (tasdiq) by the heart. Kufr, then, consists of ignorance (jahl) of what is required to be known in faith, or denial (takdhib) of it. Furthermore, turning away (i'rad) from reflection (nazar) on tawhid is kufr because it entails ignorance. Similarly, doubt (shakk) and mere opinion (zann) negate knowledge. And imitation (taqlid) is also considered kufr by al-Qadi and his followers among the majority (jumhur). Sanusi then directs the reader to note that al-Qadi indeed attributes kufr to the one who turns away from reflection (mu'rid) and the imitator (muqallid). The passage ends with editorial footnotes clarifying the text of the citation.

Key Terms
تقليد
معرفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A person might think that simply claiming belief or having a general faith without reflection suffices for true iman.
Correction: Sanusi shows that thinking one knows without understanding how one knows is not true knowledge. Many are in taqlid without realizing it, and such a state does not qualify as ma'rifa.

Checkpoint
According to the imam quoted by Sanusi, what does it mean to truly know?
A) To have memorized the creeds.
B) To know how one knows what one knows.
C) To follow a scholar blindly.
D) To have good intentions.

Key Takeaway
True knowledge (ma'rifa) requires conscious understanding, not blind imitation (taqlid).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed you truly know something about your faith simply because you've always believed it, without ever questioning how you came to that knowledge? How would you respond to the imam's test: 'Whoever thinks he knows and does not know how he knows, has not known'?
?
In what area of your life might you be in the degree of taqlid (blind imitation) while imagining you have true understanding? What would it take to move from taqlid to ma'rifa?
?
The passage says that a rational person knows his own state. Do you honestly know your own state regarding the creeds? Are you content with 'just believing' without evidence, or have you sought to know how you know? What step will you take today to gain true knowledge (ma'rifa) of at least one of the aqeedah principles?

Faith and Disbelief

The Reality of Faith and Disbelief According to al-Qadi
Sanusi presents al-Qadi's definition of iman and kufr via Ibn al-Tilmisani.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Qadi's definition of iman and kufr, and why turning away from reflection, doubt, guesswork, and imitation are considered kufr.

Core Idea
Faith is inner knowledge and affirmation; anything that negates that is kufr.

Sanusi builds on the previous lesson by citing al-Qadi's authoritative definition. According to al-Qadi, legal faith is based on inner knowledge and affirmation, and disbelief includes not only ignorance and denial but also turning away from reflection, doubt, mere opinion, and imitation. This lesson unpacks that critical position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage from al-Qadi's quote
Read the Arabic passage that starts with 'وهذا الذي حملنا عليه' to the end. It contains the definition of iman and the categories of kufr.
The passage includes a citation from Sharaf al-Din ibn al-Tilmisani.
2
Identify al-Qadi's definition of iman
Sanusi quotes al-Qadi: the reality of legal faith (iman shar'i) reduces to inner knowledge (ma'rifa) and affirmation (tasdiq) by the heart.
This is the foundation.
3
List the categories of kufr
Kufr includes: (1) ignorance of what must be known, (2) denial, (3) turning away from reflection on tawhid, (4) doubt, (5) mere opinion, and (6) imitation. All these negate the required knowledge.
The passage specifically mentions i'rad, shakk, zann, and taqlid.
4
Understand why these are kufr
Each of these states prevents or negates the inner knowledge and affirmation that constitute iman. For example, turning away from reflection leads to ignorance; doubt and opinion lack certainty; imitation lacks understanding.
This is a key point in Ahl al-Sunna theology.
5
Note the attribution to al-Qadi and the majority
Sanusi points out that al-Qadi and the majority (jumhur) hold this position, and he himself accepts it.
This shows the authority behind this definition.

?

Faith and Disbelief

Faith as Knowledge: The Qadi's Rulings on Taqlid and I'rad
Sanusi defines faith as knowledge and affirmation, and judges taqlid and i'rad as disbelief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that according to the Qadi and the majority of Sunni scholars, true faith consists of knowledge and affirmation in the heart, and that taqlid (blind imitation), i'rad (turning away from reflection), doubt, and ignorance are considered forms of disbelief.

Core Idea
Faith is not mere imitation; it requires heartfelt knowledge and affirmation.

In this passage, Sanusi clarifies the essential nature of faith and what constitutes disbelief. He presents the view of the Qadi al-Baqillani that real faith is heartfelt knowledge and affirmation, while ignorance, denial, turning away from reflection, doubt, and even blind imitation are forms of disbelief. This lesson unpacks these crucial distinctions and their implications for the believer.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the overall flow, then read the English translation to grasp the meaning. Notice the shift from discussing the individual's own state to the Qadi's definition.
2
Identify the Qadi's definition of faith
Find where the Qadi defines the reality of legal faith (iman shar'i). Note that he defines it as knowledge (ma'rifa) and affirmation (tasdiq) in the heart.
3
List the categories of disbelief
In the Qadi's statement, several things are said to be disbelief: ignorance, denial, i'rad (turning away), doubt/assumption, and taqlid. Write them down.
4
Reflect on the significance of taqlid
Consider why taqlid (imitation without understanding) is considered disbelief according to the Qadi. How does this challenge common assumptions?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا كله في حق الغيرِ المظهر للإيمانِ . وأما الإنسانُ في نفسِهِ : فهو أعرفُ بحالِهِ إِنْ كَانَ عاقلاً ، ومِنَ الجَهَلَةِ مَنْ لم يعرفُ حالَ نفسِهِ ، فهو في درجة التقليد المختلف فيها ، ويَتوهَّمُ أنَّهُ في درجةِ المعرفة ، ولهذا قال بعضُ الأئمةِ : مَنْ ظنَّ أنَّه عرف ولم يدر كيف عرف .. فلم يعرف ومنهم مَنْ لم يتقن العقائد ولو بدرجةِ التقليد ، وهو كثير ، وهذا الذي حملنا عليه قول القاضي ؛ مِنْ أنَّ مراده بالمؤمن : المؤمنُ عند الله تعالى وفي شرعِهِ ، لا مَنْ نُطلق عليه نحنُ لفظ المؤمن بناءً على الظاهر ، قد صَرَّحَ بمعناه شرفُ الدين بنُ التلمساني في ( شرح المعالم ) ، حيث تَعرَّضَ لِمَنْ يُحكَمُ عليهِ بالإيمانِ ولَمَنْ يُحكَمُ عليهِ بالكفرِ ، فنقل عن القاضي أنَّ حقيقةَ الإيمان الشرعي ترجع إلى المعرفة والتصديقِ بالقلبِ ، قال : ( فالكفر يرجع إلى الجهل بما شُرِطَ علمه في الإيمان إجماعاً ، أو التكذيب به(۱) ، وكذلك الإعراضُ عنِ النظر في التوحيدِ كفَرٌ ؛ لِمَا يلزمُهُ مِنَ الجهل ، وكذلكَ الشك والظنُّ ؛ فإنَّهما يستلزمانِ انتفاء المعرفة ، والتقليد عند القاضي ومَنْ تابعَهُ مِنَ الجمهور كذلك ((۲) فانظر عزوَهُ كفَرَ المُعرِضِ عن النظر والمقلد إلى القاضي والجمهور . . يُنبئُكَ أنَّ القاضي والجمهور لا يمنعان وجودهما ، بل إيمانهما
All of this is regarding the one who does not manifest faith. As for the person himself, he is more knowledgeable of his own state if he is rational. And among the ignorant are those who do not know their own state, so he is in the degree of taqlid (imitation) which is disputed, and he imagines that he is in the degree of knowledge. For this reason, one of the imams said: 'Whoever thinks that he knows but does not know how he knows, then he does not know.' And among them are those who have not mastered the doctrines even at the level of taqlid, and they are many. And this is what compelled us to accept the saying of the Qadi (al-Baqillani) that his intention by 'believer' is the believer in the sight of Allah Almighty and according to His law, not the one to whom we apply the term 'believer' based on outward appearance. Sharaf al-Din ibn al-Tilimsani has explicitly stated its meaning in (Sharh al-Ma'alim), where he discussed who is judged to be a believer and who is judged to be a disbeliever. He narrated from the Qadi that the reality of legal faith returns to knowledge and affirmation in the heart. He said: 'Disbelief returns to ignorance of what is required to be known in faith by consensus, or denial of it. Likewise, turning away from reflection on tawhid is disbelief because of the ignorance it entails. Likewise, doubt and assumption necessitate the absence of knowledge. And taqlid, according to the Qadi and those who follow him from the majority, is likewise.' So observe his attribution of the disbelief of the one who turns away from reflection and the muqallid to the Qadi and the majority. It tells you that the Qadi and the majority do not deny their existence; rather, their faith...
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage is a discussion about the nature of faith (iman) and the rulings on taqlid (blind imitation) and i'rad (turning away from reflection). Sanusi begins by stating that the previous discussion applies to one who does not manifest faith. Then he turns to the person regarding himself: if he is rational, he knows his own state. But some ignorant people do not know their own state, so they are in the degree of taqlid, which is a matter of disagreement, and they mistakenly think they have knowledge. An imam is quoted: whoever thinks he knows but does not know how he knows, then he does not know. Then Sanusi mentions that many have not mastered the doctrines even at the level of taqlid. This led to accepting the Qadi's (al-Baqillani's) view that 'believer' in his discussion refers to the believer in God's sight and according to His law, not according to outward appearance. Sanusi then cites Sharaf al-Din ibn al-Tilimsani's explanation from Sharh al-Ma'alim. Ibn al-Tilimsani narrates the Qadi's definition: the reality of legal faith is knowledge (ma'rifa) and affirmation (tasdiq) in the heart. Disbelief returns to ignorance of what must be known in faith by consensus, or denial of it. He adds that turning away from reflection on tawhid is disbelief because it entails ignorance. Doubt and assumption also necessitate lack of knowledge. Taqlid, according to the Qadi and the majority, is likewise considered disbelief. The passage concludes by noting that the Qadi and the majority do not deny the existence of such people as believers in some sense, but rather their faith is not valid; the sentence is cut off but implies that their faith is not accepted.

Key Terms
إيمان
تقليد
إعراض
معرفة
تصديق
كفر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Blind imitation (taqlid) is sufficient for being a believer.
Correction: The Qadi and the majority of scholars consider taqlid to be disbelief because it lacks the necessary knowledge and affirmation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the reality of legal faith (iman shar'i) according to the Qadi?
A) Verbal profession
B) Knowledge and affirmation in the heart
C) Actions
D) Imitation of ancestors

Key Takeaway
True faith requires personal knowledge and affirmation, not mere imitation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you have genuine knowledge of the core beliefs of your faith, or have you merely inherited them? What evidence in your heart proves that you know, rather than just assume?
?
If to turn away from reflection (i‘rad) on tawhid is considered disbelief, how have you personally engaged with the rational proofs for God’s existence and oneness? Is there any area where you have avoided deep reflection, thinking that rote belief suffices?
?
The passage states that whoever thinks they know but does not know how they know, actually does not know. Can you articulate, in clear words, how you came to know Allah? If not, what does that reveal about the state of your faith?

Ibn Dihaq's Classification of Shirk in Attributing Actions
Ibn Dihaq enumerates two types of shirk in attributing action: to celestial spheres and to natural causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Dihaq's threefold classification of shirk related to attributing actions to other than Allah, with emphasis on the first two types: to celestial spheres and to natural causes.

Core Idea
Attributing independent action to any created cause is a form of shirk.

This passage from Ibn Dihaq's commentary on al-Irshad presents a crucial classification of shirk. He identifies a type: attributing action to other than Allah, and then subdivides it into three kinds. We will focus on the two kinds explicitly mentioned in this excerpt: attributing effects to celestial spheres and to natural causes. Understanding these helps distinguish between tawhid and common forms of hidden shirk.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation, to grasp the classification and its context.
Pay attention to the description of each type.
2
Identify the two types and their followers
Note how Ibn Dihaq describes the first type (attribution to celestial spheres) as specific to philosophers, and the second type (attribution to natural causes) as followed by many common Muslims.
Consider why he calls them 'blind of heart'.
3
Reflect on the author's additional comment
The author of the benefit adds that even jurists may fall into this blind imitation. Think about the relevance of this warning today.

Benefit: In quoting Ibn Dihāq's words about one who attributes action to other than the Exalted. Ibn Dihāq said in his commentary on al-Irshād, when he discussed the types of shirk: (Another type of shirk is the attribution of action to other than Allah, the Glorified). He said: (This type has three kinds: first: attributing action to the celestial spheres, that they influence the lower world with effects on bodies, plants, and compounds, and that some are generated from others. This type is specific to the philosopher and those who follow him from their common folk—blind of heart, blind to every benefit, because they disbelieved in Allah out of imitation. Second: what is attributed of the actions of some to others, such that fire burns, food satiates, clothing covers, and so forth from linking habitual events until they thought them necessary. And that is a misguidance in which the philosopher is followed by many of the common Muslims.) I say: Rather, many of the jurists who occupy themselves with what does not concern them of the sciences are also blind to their guidance. (1) The verse is in al-Basīṭ meter. According to al-ʿAkkārī in his gloss: (The verse is by the righteous saint Sayyidī ʿAbd al-Ḥaqq al-Ishbīlī the verifier, he said it concerning one who disguises himself with Islam while adhering to the philosophy school.) And al-Ḥāmidī said in his glosses: (This verse is by Sayyidī ʿAbd al-Ḥaqq al-Ishbīlī, appropriate for his statement: 'and those who follow him from their common folk' because they are the ones who disbelieved in Allah in imitation of their leaders).
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage is a classification of shirk (associating partners with Allah) according to Ibn Dihāq in his commentary on al-Irshād. It is presented as a 'benefit' (فائدة). Ibn Dihāq identifies a type of shirk: attributing action (الفعل) to other than Allah. He then divides this into three subtypes: (1) attributing action to the celestial spheres (الأفلاك), claiming they independently affect the lower world in bodies, plants, and compounds, with generation from one another—this is the view of philosophers and their blind followers; (2) attributing effects to natural causes (الأسباب), like fire burning, food satiating, clothing covering, which people come to consider necessary (واجبة) rather than habitual—this misguidance is followed by many common Muslims; (3) a third type (implied but not detailed in the quoted excerpt—the text mentions 'الثالث'? Actually the OCR says 'الثاني : ...' and the third is not explicitly given? The quote says 'ثلاثة أنواع أحدها ... الثاني : ...' but does not mention the third in this excerpt. Possibly the third is mentioned elsewhere. As given, only two are listed. However, the author of the 'فائدة' (the writer of the benefit) then adds his own comment ('قلت') that many jurists (المتفقهة) also fall into this blind imitations. The footnote clarifies a poetic line that appears in the quote about being 'blind of heart'.

الأفلاك

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that attributing effects to natural causes is not shirk if one believes Allah is the ultimate cause.
Correction: Ibn Dihāq considers any independent attribution of action to a created cause as shirk, because it ascribes independent causation to creation.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Dihāq, which group is specifically associated with attributing action to the celestial spheres?
A) The common Muslims
B) The philosophers (الفيلسوف) and their followers
C) The jurists (المتفقهة)
D) The mystics

Key Takeaway
Attributing independent causation to any created entity, whether celestial spheres or natural causes, is a form of shirk that violates tawhid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'the fire burned me' or 'the medicine cured me,' do you truly believe in your heart that Allah alone caused these effects, or do you unwittingly attribute independent power to these means? How would your speech change if you were fully conscious of tawhid in every action?
?
Ibn Dihāq warns that even jurists can be blind to this subtle shirk. In what areas of your own life— your profession, your habits, your daily routines—do you unconsciously treat created causes as independent agents, forgetting that Allah is the sole Creator of all actions?
?
The philosophers' shirk is said to stem from blind imitation (taqlīd). Are there beliefs about causality or natural laws that you have absorbed from your society without examining them in light of the Qur'an and Sunnah? What would it take for you to uproot those assumptions and replace them with true tawhid?

Why Christians Were Declared Kafir: Attributes vs. Gods
Clarification that Christians were declared kafir for asserting three gods, not merely for affirming attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Christians were declared disbelievers because they affirmed three gods, not merely because they affirmed divine attributes like Essence, Knowledge, and Life.

Core Idea
Affirming divine attributes is not kufr; claiming they are separate gods is.

Some might think that simply affirming attributes such as Knowledge and Life as distinct realities leads to disbelief. But the author of this passage corrects that misunderstanding. He points to the Quranic verse that condemns those who say Allah is the third of three, showing that the sin is polytheism, not the affirmation of attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the author's correction
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice that the author directly refutes a previous claim with the word 'khatta' (error).
This is a common point of confusion in interfaith discussions.
2
Identify the two positions
Identify what the mistaken claim says (takfir due to attributes) and what the author asserts as the real reason (takfir due to three gods).
3
Examine the Quranic evidence
Look at the verse cited: Q 5:73. Understand how it directly supports the author's point that the disbelief is in saying Allah is one of three.
The verse explicitly labels such a statement as kufr.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قولهم : كُفِّرَتِ النصارى بإثباتِهم الذات والعلم والحياة) فخطأ; إذ لم يكن تكفيرهم بمُجرَّدِ إثباتِ ذلكَ ، بل بإثباتهم آلهة ثلاثة ; على ما قال تعالى: ﴿لَقَدْ كَفَرَ الَّذِينَ قَالُوا إِنَّ اللَّهَ ثَالِثُ ثَلَاثَةِ ﴾ [المائدة : ٧٣] ،
And as for their saying: 'The Christians were declared disbelievers (kuffiru) for their affirmation of the Essence, Knowledge, and Life' — it is an error. For their being declared disbelievers was not merely due to affirming that, but due to their affirming three gods, as He (Exalted) said: 'They have certainly disbelieved who say, "Indeed, Allah is the third of three."' [al-Ma'idah 5:73].
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage is a correction of a mistaken claim. The author refutes the idea that Christians were declared disbelievers (kafir) because they affirmed that God has attributes of Essence (dhat), Knowledge ('ilm), and Life (hayah). He states that this is an error (khatta'). The real reason for their takfir is that they affirmed three gods (aliha thalatha), as explicitly stated in the Qur'an (5:73). The author thus draws a crucial distinction: affirming divine attributes is not in itself kufr; rather, it is the polytheistic interpretation of those attributes as separate deities that constitutes disbelief.

Key Terms

نصارى (Nasara)
ذات (dhat)
علم ('ilm)
حياة (hayah)
آلهة ثلاثة (aliha thalatha)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Christians were declared kafir simply because they affirmed attributes like Knowledge and Life as distinct realities.
Correction: The author clarifies that the takfir was specifically for their belief in three gods (trinity), not for affirming attributes.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say it is an error to claim that Christians were declared kafir for affirming the Essence, Knowledge, and Life?
A) Because Christians do not affirm those attributes.
B) Because the takfir was for affirming three gods, not for affirming attributes themselves.
C) Because affirming attributes is always permissible.
D) Because the Quran does not mention Christians.

Key Takeaway
Affirming divine attributes is not disbelief; declaring them as separate gods is what constitutes kufr.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
This passage warns against misidentifying the reason for takfir. Is there any part of your own religious practice where you have accepted a superficial explanation without verifying its truth? What concrete step will you take to investigate it?
?
The error here is confusing attributes with separate gods. In your daily life, do you ever confuse what is true about Allah with what is merely your own assumption? How can you realign your understanding with what the Quran and authentic sources actually teach?

Reason Cannot Determine Good and Evil Without Revelation
Sanusi argues that reason alone cannot judge moral value, and that obligation comes only through revelation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why, according to Sanusi, reason alone cannot determine moral value and why revelation is necessary for knowing obligation and prohibition.

Core Idea
Without revelation, reason cannot settle moral judgments.

Before diving into Sanusi's argument, consider a foundational question: can our minds independently figure out what is good or evil, or do we need divine guidance? Sanusi addresses this directly, challenging the view that reason suffices. In this passage, he explains why revelation is indispensable for moral knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage builds from a reminder about God to a final doctrinal statement.
2
Identify the main claim
Sanusi states that we do not know the obligation of faith or the prohibition of disbelief except after revelation arrives. Find this statement in the text.
This is the conclusion of the argument.
3
Follow the reasoning
Trace Sanusi's reasoning: He first notes that there is no inherent preference among actions relative to God. Then he criticizes those who claim reason can perceive good and evil without revelation. Even if granted, reason cannot be certain due to conflicting considerations.
The argument has two parts: a negation of reason's ability and a positive statement about revelation.
4
Reflect on the Ash'ari position
The passage ends with a reference to the Ash'ari doctrine that all actions are directly from God. Consider how this underpins the rejection of independent moral reasoning.
The final line is unclear, but the main point is that nothing other than God affects actions.
5
Ask: why does this matter?
Think about the implications for faith and ethics. If reason cannot determine obligation, then revelation becomes the sole source of moral guidance.
This connects to the ongoing debate between Ash'aris and Mu'tazilis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا عرفت بما ذُكِرَ عدم رجحان بعض الأفعال على بعض بالنسبة إليه تعالى .. عرفت جهالة مَنْ تسَوَّرَ على الغيبِ ورأى أنَّ العقل يتوصل دون شرع إلى إدراك الحسن والقبيح عندَهُ جلَّ وعلا ، على أنَّهُ لو سُلّم لهم ذلك جدلاً لم يجزم العقل بشيء ؛ لتعارض أوجُهِ مِنَ النظر في ذلك مُتضادة فإذاً ؛ لم نعرف وجوبَ الإيمان ولا تحريم الكفران إلا بعد مجيء الشرع لمَّا حقَّقَ أَنَّ مذهب أهلِ السنَّةِ أَنَّ الأفعال كلَّها مسندة إلى اللهِ تعالى ابتداءً مِنْ غيرِ واسطةٍ ، لا تأثير لغيرِهِ في شيء منها .. لزمَ أَنَّ الأفعال تعالى لأنهما لفظان موهمان للمُحال ، ولم يرد بهما سماع
And if you have understood from what was mentioned the lack of preference of some actions over others relative to Him, exalted, then you have recognized the ignorance of one who intrudes into the unseen and thinks that reason can, without revelation, perceive the good and the evil with Him, mighty and sublime. Moreover, even if that were granted to them as a hypothetical, reason would not be certain of anything due to the conflicting aspects of consideration in that which are contradictory. Therefore, we know neither the obligation of faith nor the prohibition of disbelief except after the coming of revelation. [And] when He established that the doctrine of Ahl al-Sunnah is that all actions are attributed to God, exalted, from the beginning without any intermediary, and no other has any effect in any of them, it follows that the actions [are attributed] to the Exalted because they are two terms that suggest the impossible, and no transmitted evidence has come concerning them.
Source pages p. 535

Commentary
This passage demonstrates that reason cannot independently determine moral values without revelation. Sanusi begins by reminding that there is no inherent preference among actions with respect to God, so anyone who claims that reason can discern good and evil (al-husn wa al-qubh) without revelation is ignorant. Even if one were to grant the Mu'tazilite assumption, reason still cannot reach certainty because moral judgments are subject to conflicting perspectives. Hence, the obligation of faith and prohibition of disbelief are known only through shar' (revelation). The passage closes by reiterating the Ash'ari position that all actions are directly from God, implying that any description suggesting deficiency (like safah or 'abath) is inapplicable to God, and such terms have no scriptural basis.

Key Terms
الحسن
القبيح
التحسين والتقبيح
الشرع
أهل السنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Reason alone can tell us that faith is obligatory and disbelief is prohibited.
Correction: Sanusi argues that without revelation, we cannot know these obligations; reason cannot achieve certainty due to conflicting considerations.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, why can reason not determine the obligation of faith without revelation?
A) Because reason is inherently flawed
B) Because there is no inherent preference among actions, and moral considerations are contradictory
C) Because God did not create reason for that purpose
D) Because revelation cancels reason

Key Takeaway
Reason cannot determine moral obligation; only revelation tells us that faith is obligatory and disbelief is prohibited.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a moral dilemma, do you typically rely more on your own reasoning or on the Qur'an and Sunnah? What does this passage reveal about the danger of trusting your own judgment without revelation?
?
Think of a time when you justified a sin by reasoning that it wasn't 'that bad' or that everyone does it. How does Sanusi's argument that reason cannot produce certainty about what is good or evil challenge that justification?
?
If you truly believe that only revelation tells you that faith is obligatory and disbelief is prohibited, how should that change the way you prioritize learning the Qur'an and Sunnah over your own opinions? What specific action will you take this week to align your moral compass with revelation?

The Murji'ah Doctrine on Punishment
The author presents the Murji'ah doctrine on punishment and their first proof from the Quran.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Murji'ah claim that punishment is only for disbelievers and their first proof from the Quran.

Core Idea
The Murji'ah exempt Muslim sinners from punishment based on selective Quranic verses.

The Murji'ah hold an extreme view on punishment, claiming that only disbelievers will face Allah's wrath. In this lesson, we examine the core of their doctrine and their first proof from the Quran. Understanding their position is essential before we see how it is refuted.

Guided Path
1
Read the Murji'ah doctrine
Read the first sentence carefully: the Murji'ah believe punishment is only fitting for the disbeliever, not the Muslim. They also negate punishment for those who die with major sins without repentance.
2
Examine their first proof
Look at the verse they cite (al-Nahl 16:27) and its support from Aal 'Imran 3:192. They argue that disgrace (khizy) is associated with disbelievers, so punishment is specific to them.
Notice how they use the word 'khizy' to link to entering the Fire.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider what this doctrine means for accountability. Does it align with other Quranic verses? This will be addressed in later lessons.
